RATING: 7.5
A thematic and surprisingly original take on the tried and true worker placement genre.

As per usual, the goal of the game is to score the most points by game end, and there are many ways to score these points.  You start the game with a player mat which has a clock of sorts that marks the passage of time depending upon the actions that you take.  Your actions consist of taking an action cube (which is a currency of sorts and which comes in 5 colours) and then performing that action, and depending on the action you may also have to spend time and/or grain which is another form of currency.  Actions consist of such things as: currying favour in the city council, entering the priesthood, selling goods at the market, travelling/adventuring, crafting goods or paying a craftsman to make them for you, and having a child.  The trick is that your workers have an expiry date, and when their time is up, they die and are removed from the game.  The neat catch is that in addition to this being a trigger for game end (the cemetary or book of legends fills up with the deceased) it can also be one of the many ways to score points- if you manage to die at the right time and place you can put your deceased family member in a book of legends which is worth more points the more family members you get in there.

The ways to score points are myriad: dying (appropriately), adventuring, working your way up the ranks in the church, selling goods at the market, maintaining a high position in city council.  The game can play quickly or long depending on what players are working towards.  You can win with many or few family members, the scalability is great for 2-4 (and now 5 with the expansion), and the artwork is nice and the theme fits nicely.  

Own the [boardgame=136223]Village Inn[/boardgame] and [boardgame=155996]Village Port[/boardgame] expansions and the [boardgame=124118]Customers[/boardgame], [boardgame=166592]Customers 2[/boardgame] and [boardgame=168122]Schatzkiste[/boardgame] promos.  The Village Inn is an easy add-on and should be considered essential.  It adds components for up to 5 players and the game works well with 5, but it also adds a new location and set of options (the Beer maker and Inn/Tavern)- you can produce beer with one action (a craftsman action) and go to the tavern with another, and with this action you gain access to unique power cards (personalities) that give one-time in game bonuses or end game scoring bonuses.  Basically this option opens up more paths to explore without being unbalanced.

RATING: 9
A rather unique but mechanically simple game. In village, you strive to build the most successful family, but time is working against you and you never have quite as much influence as you want. It is wonderfully thematic although occasionally bizarre in hilarious ways. Like for example, you can expand the family even if you don't have two family members, which inspired jokes around the table about how we're actually stealing babies dumped off at the steps of the church. Similarly, you can't help but wonder why the wagons keep on breaking... is your traveler racing them or something? There is slight disbalance that makes it hard for the travel strategy to win, but this issue is completely resolved by village port. Village Port also adds life goals, which are a perfect complement that makes the game yet more thematic and provides direction. I have also played village inn, which is very enjoyable and adds a new layer to the game. This is a game I will never play again without at least the village port expansion and I prefer to play it with both expansions are once, even with new players. The expansions bring this game up to a 10.

RATING: 6
This beautiful complex worker placement game feels like a step forward from Stone Age and Ninjato, with its multiple paths to victory, story-evoking theme, and clever new mechanic whereby your workers die of old age scoring you victory points. It's a bit of a time investment, but doesn't outstay its welcome. I think this is a really excellent new game that brings something unique to the genre.

Update: Didn't hold my interest in the long run. Enjoyed 6 games very much, but they all started to feel very similar, despite trying the different strategies. Traded away, for Troyes, which is a fabulous game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
a unique take on action-selection, resource management, worker placement game. Quite similar to Orleans. You play as 4 generations of family, looking to make their mark in the village.
On your turn, you can choose between crafting goods (yellow), adding/calling back family member (purple in front of the temple), harvest in farmyard (purple), becoming priest in the temple (brown), becoming politician in council chamber (red), travelling (green) or serving customers in market by selling goods (blue). You take cube (green, pink, orange, brown, black) from the action spot as resource to your personal farmyard (if black cube, just advance your time tracker 2 spaces) and may do the action. A round will finish when all cubes are removed from the main board. Then, the mass ritual is performed to pick who will become priest in the temple.
The most unique & interesting element in this boardgame that piqued my interest to buy it is that one of your family members would die each time your time tracker crosses the bridge. You should plan their death well to make sure they would be put into the village chronicle & earn points by the end of the game. If not, they will just be buried in the normal grave behind the temple. Once either the chronicle/grave is full, the game ends.
I like this game. It does feel like you are controlling a family of meeples. My first game was around 3-4 full rounds, maybe 20-30 minutes per player. It's not as point salad as Feld games as I thought. It has relaxing theme & nice pacing. Just enough randomization for replayability. Certainly a keeper.

The meanest mechanism in the game would absolutely be the market, taking the action before your opponent can gather enough resources to be sold to the customers. Always love how vulnerable the meeples are in the game haha

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A well done action drafting game with a built in "mortality mechanic". In Village you try to ensure your family members die famous for the right things at the right time so that they are remembered in the village chronical and earn you points or that they end up in the right careers at the right time and love until the end of the game to also earn you points. The game is essentially about promoting your family in a village through different means and exploitation of resources and or time (time is a form of currency in the game and dries the mortality of your meeples). The more successful your family members are in certain careers while living, or remembered in death for what they contributed to the village, the better your score will be at the end of the game. Always worth a play in my mind.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Interesting gateway type worker placement/get resources/take actions to score points game with an unusual twist: a valid strategy is to kill off your workers! That's because filling up the scroll of honourable dead (or whatever it's called) gives you points.

Fun for non-hardcore gamers as it is easy to pick up strategy as you basically look at what others are doing and try to do something different.

Of the point generating spaces, the market seems to be needed in most games so there may be some "sameness" developing over time.

Still, a great game to play with family and friends who are casual gamers or may be just getting into more mid-weight games.

One major caveat: if you are colour deficient you will have a hard time with the board and the cubes. The orange and pink cube representations on the board are strangely similar and I have a difficult time telling them apart, especially on the travel section of the board.

10/09/13:  I'm trying to figure out why I don't like this one.  It's a euro.  It's got an interconnected engine.  The engine is variable and there are a few different directions.  I *should* like this more than I do.

Maybe it's because the different engines are unequal.  Maybe because I haven't seen someone win without going heavy on the market.  Maybe it's because there actually aren't that many different engines (travel, market, chronicle, guys on the board).  Maybe it's because the first thing we do each round is the family action to get more guys.  Maybe it's because putting the wrong guy at the wrong place is ridiculously punishing.  Maybe it's because the end of the game just rushes into place.

I dunno, but I really am not a fan of this game.  I'll play it, but it's just not for me.

RATING: 3.3
:cry: :snore: :snore: :snore:  Long and dry. I'm not a huge fan of economic resources-to-VP style euros, although this is probably the least unpleasant of the ones I've played. I might like it more if it actually played out in the advertised 60-90 minutes rather than the 180 minutes of hell my first game of this was... but I probably wouldn't want to risk the time on trying it again when I already have a half-dozen excellent and beloved games in the 2+ hour range that I'd rather play. 

I was originally going to call this an exquisitely crafted, finely-tuned, well-polished turd, but there's actually a decent game in there. It just takes way too much time and effort to extract that decent game.

Game owners, do yourself a favor, sticker your meeples before introducing others to this game, because nobody with stubby gamer fingers wants to start game night with a delicate 20 minute arts-and-crafts project. It just set a tone of "doing tedious things" that never really left when I played this game. 

Not an awful game but it just rubs my brain in the wrong way -- I have to go against all of my INTJ intuition to play it, and it makes it sort of mentally unpleasant.

RATING: 8.85
Rules-wise, it's not exactly complicated; but boy, it can be fiddly! There is never a “This is the best action for me right now” moment: each action is but a step in a process, and each action will, in the short term, limit subsequent actions for both you and your opponents. And your choices may end up leading you in directions that you had not foreseen!  This is due to the randomness of the cube selection each round. No, Village is not a heavily random game; but what randomness it has results in excruciating decisions. It is a wonderful balance of strategy and tactics. You need a strategy, definitely; but you need to strategize keeping in mind the rapidly changing conditions created by you and your opponents. At least once a round (whether you end up winning or losing), you will be convinced that you’ve been doing the wrong thing! In spite of all that I’ve just said, though, the game is surprisingly lacking in analysis paralysis.
Negatives? Well, the colour selection of the cubes is appalling, and makes the game nigh near impossible for the colour blind, and is even a bit of a challenge at times for the rest of us! Also, the game end phase can be a long drawn-out affair, since someone has to trigger it, and it’s pretty easy avoiding triggering if you suspect that you still need to earn more VP’s.
Not heavily themed, except for the aging-of-the-meeple mechanic. If you find it too dry, play it with the “give-the-deceased-meeple-a-eulogy” variant.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Lots of ways to score, resource cubes to spend, always wanting to do more than you are able, minimal player interaction (the usual taking stuff before an opponent), and a rustic medieval German setting.  If only there had been buildings to build, this would have been the archetypal euro game.  

That's perhaps a little unfair on Village.  The idea of time passing and death periodically claiming your workers is quite nicely implemented.  The sort of inverse worker placement - you take off a cube to perform an action - works well to limit the number of times each action can be taken and create tension.  And the game is quite faithful to its hackneyed theme.  Would I play it again?  Ye-e-es.

Production quality is excellent and the artwork is pleasingly evocative.

RATING: 8.7
A real gem here.  There are a lot of underlying systems at work, but they come together in a surprisingly wonderful way.  I am impressed at how smooth game play is given the variety of things going on.  This is a true strategy game--you will have to pick a path or two and really invest in it/them to do well. The death mechanic, which likely draws most people to the game in the first place (it did me), is unique and compelling.  The game is a bit daunting at first, given everything that's going on, but you'll figure it out a lot sooner than you might initially think.  The farmyard "profession", and how harvesting worked with respect to meeple placement on specific areas of the farmyard (or lackthereof, as it turns out), was a bit confusing for us at first, but other than that there were no real confusing aspects in the learning process.

RATING: 9
This is one of my favorites. It's a euro style game with worker placement mechanics. Your meeples is borned in generations, and they die during the game. Just make shure they did something usefull while living and that they be honored to be written in the chronicle after death.
The game has different possible strategies to win, and you have to look out for what other players are doing.
Every turn you take a resource from a place on the board, and if you like do the actions possible at that place. I love how this makes you choose between what's most important; getting the right resource, doing a certain action or avoid another player from having that action or resource. Sometimes it's a great idea to get some plague, just to get rid of granddad.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
If the theme of killing off your old family members doesn't entice you... the gameplay just might. Mechanically speaking, this is a worker placement euro. But when players take actions, there is an additional cost not seen in most games... time. When you circle your board in time, you need to kill off the lowest value family member. 

Now, killing off family members can be both a bad thing and a good thing. On the negative side, many worker-placement tracks cost resources/time/actions to move up on... and loosing that particular worker can be especially hurtful. But on the positive side, there is a strong race element to kill family members in specific spots, which can be significant at the end of the game. Overall, it is very situational and forces you to plan ahead. Furthermore, workers dying is how the end-game is triggered, which allows players to manipulate the speed of the game based on what their opponents are doing. 

Overall, the game provides interesting tactical and strategic decisions within a rule set that isn't too difficult (medium to medium-light) and quite eloquent (very few exceptions to the rules). I like it quite a bit =).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Four games now.  First game I just couldn’t get my mind round it.  I’ve found others not liking it first play also, but telling them that was my experience, but that I am now enjoying it quite a bit.

I must admit I’ve stuck with the same strategy each time, five deaths (so accelerating deaths where possible – never buying with cubes, but taking time, and birthing workers from the board to get the deaths in the right places) coupled with completing the countryside.  So far its won each of the games so I guess I should try something different next time.  It’s also interesting that with this tactic, all the number one workers die, so workers placed early in the council and church won’t survive to the game end.  All good stuff.  Recommended.

Akár nagyon gyorsan is véget érhet egyébként egy parti. Nekem még jobban tetszene, ha nem lenne benne a zsákhúzogatós szerencsefaktor a kápolnánál, illetve ha nem lenne ennyire esetleges a befejezés időpontja. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Village is a very good game currently flying under the radar on BGG (it wasn't ready for Essen 2011, and hasn't had an US release yet, so that is quite understandable...): it could easily be considered one of the best medium weight euros of the last year!

Its core mechanic is worker placement (a favorite of mine), with a couple of interesting twists:
1. action selection and (part of the) resource acquisition are interwined and performed contestually, choosing one of the available resource cubes from one of the action spaces;
2. workers have a life span and actually die (many actions cost "hourglasses", and after paying a certain amount of time, one of the elders of your family passes away): there's some tension between the need to keep some key workers alive, and the need to bury them soon to be immortalized in the village chronicles.

The rules are well written and easy to understand and explain (after a couple of turns everyone but the most inexperienced non-gamer can play reasonably), the components are sturdy and well designed, there is a lot of ways to score points (and they appear balanced!), and lots of meaningful decisions in a reasonably short time-span.

Initial rating is 7.5, but it can go up if the game can stand repeated plays...

RATING: 10
Excellent game with deep strategy that rewards both planning ahead and flexibility.  It offers many paths to victory, all of which are remarkably well balanced, and give the game plenty of replay value.  And with its beautiful artwork and engaging theme, Village is a real home run.

This game is full of tough decisions; there are a lot of things to do in the village, but only so much you can actually accomplish before the game ends.  You must always weigh the opportunity cost of choosing one action before another because the action you don't choose might not be there on your next turn.  I should mention, however, that this tension is not accomplished in the normal worker placement way.  There is no blocking when it comes to placing your workers.  Rather there is a set amount of colored cubes in each general area of the board that denote the number of available actions, and these influence cubes double as a form of currency once they are picked up by the players.  Once all of the influence cubes are gone from a given area, that area is closed until the next round.  The game does offer one workaround to this in a pinch, but it involves spending three of your influence cubes which can be costly.

One of the strategic variables is in Village the length of the game itself, which will depend both on the number of players and on how quickly people run out their clocks and kill their workers.  If everyone uses influence cubes to accomplish their tasks, the game can be very long indeed, but if people choose to work their family members harder by using more time, the graves will fill faster and the game will end much sooner.  It is worth mentioning that in my experience the game usually lasts about 30-45 minutes per player, so the 75 minutes in the game description is a rather low estimate.

Another thing I like about this game is that the role of luck is kept to an absolute minimum.  Most of the variation will come from players responding to one anothers' strategies.  The better you can predict your opponents' strategies, the more you can plan ahead for yourself, and in some cases thwart or delay an opponent's plans.  The only downside to this is that more experienced players will most likely have a significant advantage over new players, but this is to be expected in a good strategy game.

The only surefire advice I can offer a new player is, perform family actions (grow your family) early and often, and don't let any one player dominate a single area of the board unchallenged.

RATING: 8
Nice, a good, solid worker placement game with just the right feel of "so many things I want to do, but I wont get the chance to do all of them, which do I do first?"

Often in WP games, getting extra workers early gives you a powerful advantage. The cube-taking mechanism in this mitigates that issue nicely, whilst the death mechanism gives you a powerful incentive to keep growing and reducing your workers.

Behind all that are your very familar area-majority and triangular-scoring type mechanisms (probably too many of them if truth be told) but the innovative use of workers freshened the whole thing up enough to stop the over-familiarity being an issue.

Solid game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
You can try to convince me The Village is a thematic game but there’s no way I will agree: it’s just a dry and boring euro whose theme is nothing more than a convenient wrapping for its mechanics.

That said, I can agree with the committed eurogamer that, as far as his typical euro goes, The Village is a thematic game and even sports interesting features, such as letting your grandpas die while the new generations take their place.

Nonetheless The Village is still too cubes and economy-based (undoubtedly the market is overpowered) to be an enjoyable game and it’s even less fun since it prevents you from directly interfering with your opponents.

RATING: 7.5
A well balanced euro with an interesting death mechanic. But it's somewhat dry.
What makes it strong is that the different ways to gain points seem very balanced, but you need to be efficient about what you do. Since the board state is somewhat randomized each round, you'll always need to think of a clever way to do what you wanted to or even change your strategy to adapt.

Sometimes there can be bottlenecks in your opponenent's plan. Spotting those and blocking them can quickly become important. This demands a varied approach to the game that doesn't depend on a single thing.

RATING: 7
One game, 2er.  
Village is a solid offering.  The game I played went by quickly.  As is typical of "worker placement" style games, the rounds are broken into placements that offer bite sized decisions.  Individual turns typically go pretty quickly once players have taken stock of what is on offer in a given round.  The mechanisms of the various spaces available are straightforward as well.  The whole thing comes together with just enough complexity in the interactions, and randomness in the cube draws for the rounds that it offers an interesting decision space without being overwhelming.  

Overall, I didn't come away desperate to play again, but would certainly be willing to play this.  Not exciting, but good enough to play.

RATING: 8.5
Juego de gestión de recursos con un par de mecánicas bastante originales. En primer lugar, los recursos los obtienes al escoger la opción...(en lugar de colocar un trabajador, recoger un cubito de material). En segundo lugar, el paso del tiempo (algunas acciones implican un gasto de tiempo) provoca que los trabajadores se vayan muriendo. Cada jugador lleva "una familia" con cuatro generaciones.
Hay unas cuantas acciones (artesanos, mercado, concejo, pozo, boda, iglesia) y muchas formas de conseguir sumar puntos. La gestión de la muerte de tus trabajadores también es importante, porque determina la duración de la partida y además, te permite sumar puntos.

Es un juego muy original y divertido. Muy recomendable. No es dificil de jugar y creo que le gustará a todo el mundo.

RATING: 8.5
A neat twist on worker placement, where you don't block opponents when you take an action, but actions are limited according to cubes available on the board. Having older workers die off early in the game really gives a different sense of pacing than other WP games, and forces you to think strategically, both according to which parts of the board you want to use and in terms of long term scoring. Since players are required to take cubes each turn, however, you also have to tweak your strategy once in a while in order to maximize your points. The Village Inn expansion is also great, and simple enough to teach to first time players.

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ Good medium-weight action selection (draft a ressource cube) / worker placement (invest a worker into a profession) game with some paths to victory.
++ The fact of having multiple generation of workers, that die, and to score this, is really innovative.
++ Using the notion of passing time as an alternative ressources to do stuff is really innovative as well and really well implemented in the game theme as passing time eventually kills your workers, in order of generation (oldest workers die first).
++ I really like that all the gameplay information is well blended on the board (action information are depicted clearly on the roof of buildings).
++ There are expansions available for this game (which is useful to refresh the game when we have overplayed it).


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- Although we should like everything about this game, it doesn't seem to have clicked as much as other game in this kind, probably due to the lack of an engine-building aspect (you're not building up your family, you just play them until they die).
-- The replayability seem to be limited without any expansions, as there seem to be only a few viable strategy to follow (at least in a 2-player game).
-- The colored cubes, although typical in a Euro and practical because we have to draw them blind for the setup, doesn't easily evoke the idea behind them (skill, faith, knowledge, etc.) and thus player just refer to them as brown cube, instead of Faith cube for example. It would have been awesome that they could have use some wooden disks with silkscreen on it with images that could have convey the theme better (but I know this would have increase the costs).
-- The printing on the rule book and the board is a little bit sub par (the surface is rough instead of being smooth).
-- I hate having to put sticker on game parts (but I know why they do it instead of printing on the parts).

RATING: 8
A really cool take on the worker placement mechanic, as it kind of takes the system and turns it upside down.

In reach round, action space are seeded with cubes, and on your turn, you take a cube from an action space and take that action.  When a space is out of cubes, that action can no longer be taken (I guess there is an exception to this, but in general this is true).  Additionally, the cubes you take matter, as there are several colors of cubes which you'll need as parts of the various actions.  So, in the end, not only do you have to balance which actions you need to take before they're gone, but also which cubes you'll need to make them work.

Yes, each player does have workers, but they're more of a resource that you'll need in order to do things, rather than being used in the traditional worker placement sense.  The real "worker placement" here is the taking of cubes from action spaces in order to take actions.

Under this very clever mechanism, you have your standard Euro "lots of options for scoring points and there's more to do than I'll have time to do" system.  So, the real uniqueness here is in the methodology to reach the goal.  The goals are all familiar.

Overall, just a really neat system that I really look forward to playing again.

RATING: 6
An innovative rather light eurogame. The age mechanic about when your meeples die is quite unique as you really have to plan forward as to where you want the meeples to die (so they can get in the village chronicles)!

There are lots of different ways to score victory points, which is always a plus. However, it seems that you simply cannot ignore the market if you want to win; it gives you too many VPs compared to for instance travel. At least that's what I've seen in my games, and even though it might be a coincidence, the consensus on BGG seems to be that the market is stronger than most of the other actions and that you'll often win if you concentrate on doing well there. 
Nevertheless, you need to get some meeples in the chronicles too and preferably one or two in the church etc. While you do need to do a little bit of everything, the market is where the big points are scored (it seems). Because of this, I really cannot give it a better rating than 6; games which have a superior strategy simply aren't that good after all...

The game plays equally well with all player counts and doesn't take much more than an hour to play which is pretty good considering the hard decisions you need to make during the game.

RATING: 7
Village is a very enjoyable worker placement game. The twist is that, as you draft actions, you are also drafting resources. This means you have to weigh how bad you want the action in addition to what resources you need.

The other addition is that your workers eventually "die," migrating off the board. Certain scoring opportunities require your workers to stay on the board, so you have to plan around this attrition of your workers.

It's a slick design, I enjoyed it... but it may be just a little too difficult for new gamers. The playing time is very manageable... I don't know if I'm inclined to pick this up, but I'd happily play it again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A solid worker placement game with a twist in that workers will eventually die and you'll lose their ability that has been helping you for a few rounds. It is definitely something to plan for as losing a worker in one area may hurt one of your other workers ability so you do need to spread around the board and try and do multiple things. The game took a round longer than we all thought because we were all able to stay alive when we for sure thought either the chronicle or graveyard would fill up so a little longer than I liked, but still the turns moved quickly. I was honestly a bit surprised by Village as I thought it would be pretty dry, but the death of your workers and the little bit of extra thought that goes into it proved to be fun. Definitely enjoyed this one.

RATING: 6
:sugar: :( :D :D :D Puzzly cube-pusher of the sort I am not usually wild about but this one won me over with its charm (as charming as possible for a game whose elevator pitch is "Meeple Death"), accessibility (a few pages of nicely-presented rules) and brevity (90 minutes tops if everyone's paying attention). Pretty much everyone I've sprung this on (including the decidedly non-gamer spouse) has enjoyed it. There are many sources of points and the game makes it extraordinarily difficult to do well in all of them. Finding the ideal balance is extraordinarily tricky and the opportunity-cost calculations are not insignificant. Five games in, it seems that canny use of the market and the far points on the city hall track is quite strong and travel seems quite weak, relative to the resource cost.

I think that grinding on the mathematics (i.e., thinking through all of those opportunity costs) of Village will yield positive results w/r/t good play, but the fundamental system is light enough, and the rule set is well-enough integrated into the theme, that this game is suitable for casual gamers. At least, for casual gamers of a certain bent: apart from a little bit of blocking and some race mechanics, the game is conflict-free -- it makes Agricola seem like Risk, such is its commitment to non-nastiness.

Conclusion: if you're going to play one of the current wave of Caylus-with-training-wheels games, you could do a hell of a lot worse than this.

RATING: 8
Well balanced and innovative worker placement game. Mostly about finding the right tactical plan (sequence of actions either to increase your payoff or to decrease that of your opponents. It's a standard "go where other people don't"  kind of game, but there are high-level strategic directions and combos one can follow. Overall, quite a thinky game, while not too difficult to learn. A real pleasure, especially when played when Village Inn (haven't played the Port yet).

Play with scoring market tiles as soon as you get them, no point in playing memory games...

Don't like: 
- the expansion board-lets that cover the board
- market participation (and thus crafting) is quite mandatory, but that's ok once you know it

Likes: Very interesting management of "time" that adds greatly to strategy. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Yet another worker placement game but with a lovely twist: death! In this game your workers (family members) actually die after some time - with time itself being one of the currencies of the game system. So you will have to bring new generations of workers into play while trying to let the grim reaper take your elderly family members preferably in the prime of their life (thus securing a place in the village chronicle for them). The game seems balanced and comes with very well written rules that leave no question unanswered. The theme in most parts fits the variety of different mechanisms present in the game and the graphics and game components are nicely done. Only the attempt to match the perspective of the icons on the game board with the drawings done in perspective has gone utterly wrong. Those are painful to look at. If this game wasn't limited to a maximum of 4 players it would hit the table quite often. (This is for the German eggertspiele / Pegasus (2011) edition.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very nice game. And nice twist on action selection. Choose an action [b]and[/b] a cube there. Many actions require cubes, but you often can't take the cube you want when taking the action you want, so you have to manage the balance. But be careful or your people might die. Wait, that's actually a good thing! Or is it? I dunno! Wow I stink at this game. I think I'm suffering from some cognitive dissonance between what things seem they should be worth, and what they're really worth. Such as: "getting stuff", and minor things like "keeping your people alive". Very little randomness, except for setup each round, which is the sweet-spot for me. But minus .5 for the random draw of monks at mass. That's just overly random. Although I'm not sure how I'd fix it. But minor issue.

RATING: 8.5
w/ all expansions
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Forgettable, bland, totally run-of-the-mill worker placement. Yes: removing the cubes [i]is[/i] worker placement, just not with workers. The principle of action blocking and their dearth is still the same. I suppose that if this title is your first big(ger) specimen in this genre you will be enamoured by it: a nice timing mechanism, some blocking, some randomness to keep it light and not too difficult, apparently lots of strategies... But if you're a grizzled cynical bastard like me, you'll soon see that these 'strategies', while numerous, are not really challenging besides recognising and subsequently following them. There is very little in the way of creative thinking required here. The game is very mild in the way players can cause havoc with the plans of others: only the aspect which has some players actively striving to end the game earlier than others want by forcing their villagers to die quickly causes some interesting tension. Unfortunately I have a number of other titles which execute this tension much better than The Village (Luna, Coney Island, perhaps even the nasty Grand Cru), putting the game into the class of 'fun for once every while' for me. It remains somewhat of a mystery why this title is so popular.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
This game is good. The artwork is nice and somehow wonderfully rustic (as it should be) in my opinion.

I've scored about five plays with it now and I admit I'm not very good in this game. Somehow someone else always manages to optimize their VP engine better, though I've usually been the close second. Despite this terrible flaw (heh) I've enjoyed all the plays.

The game mechanics are solid and there are multiple viable ways to victory. You won't have time to compete efficiently in all of them so you'll have to focus on one or two of them. The other players will be doing the same and thus a frantic game usually follows. The cubes are a nice touch of randomness to the game.

I rated this game a round 8. It's a good game but lacks that extra special 'oompf' to reach higher in my books.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
On the surface this looked to be another game of simple worker placement, cube pushing, and point salad.  But as we got deeper into the game, I was charmed by how it all fits together so nicely.  The mechanism of your workers being born and later dying - and trying to kill them off strategically - is awfully clever.  Now I can see why it's been so popular and won the KdJ.

Village is a little bit challenging to learn, but once you've played through it once it will be far easier to teach going forward, so I recommend learning from someone with experience.

Also - we played with the expansion (see below) and can't imagine playing without it.  So for me this is really an 8, but knowing that excellent expansion is out there to improve it nudges it up for me in the ratings.  Now I just need to get a copy!

RATING: 8
Worker placement game for 2-4 players with a nice twist: your workers will die over the course of the game and if you play it right, they will become part of the village chronicles. The game is quite accessible and takes about 10-15 minutes to explain and a 4 player games playes in 75-90 minutes (with experienced players probably 60-75).

I've played with 2,3 and 4 players and the games scales nicely to suit the player count. As I have nothing quite like it in my collection, this one is a keeper.

RATING: 7
(Sònia 2012)
Joc de gestió de treballadors ambientat a l'edat mitja.
Controles una família (fins a 4 generacions). Vas posant membres de la família en varis oficis: grangers, religiosos, polítics, viatjants, etc.

RATING: 6
A variation on the worker placement mechanic. The game flows smoothly and our first play surprised us... a lot of things we expected to happen didn't happen and several unexpected things did.

The game looks like it has a theme, but the theme is pretty weak. Those cubes (essentially) mean nothing. Just color combos.

The game suffers a bit from what I've seen called "point salad", where almost everything is worth points in some way... this makes nothing seem particularly important. (In fact, there's a strategy of killing off all your workers as quickly as possible by taking plague cubes. This, too, rewards you with points by getting your workers into the town annals quickly... and ending the game before anyone can score many points of other kinds.)

The end game also has a frustrating issue. Workers are drawn randomly from a bag. The results of this draw can very, very easily decide the winner if the game is at all close. To have a medium-weight game come down to a coin flip is annoying to me.

RATING: 8
Players run a family of villagers, working their way through village life, trying to gain prestige (victory points).  A straightforward game, but with a lot going on.  A turn is simply remove an available cube from an action pool, and take the corresponding action.  Actions are divided into craft (make stuff, or acquire livestock), agriculture (gain food), business (earn game rewards), church (prestige), travel (escalating prestige), and so on.  The cubes you take are coloured, and you have to pay back sets of colours to take other actions, so there is a cyclic synergy thing happening.  A lot of actions also take time, which is recorded on a private Thebes-like time track.  When a lap is completed, one of your village elders shuffles off to either the village chronicle (prestige points!) or a pauper's grave (nothing) - so you want to time your deaths well!  Really nice game - a beast to teach, but plays out very smoothly, and importantly, doesn't overstay it's welcome.  Very good.

RATING: 8.5
Worker placement game in which your villagers eventually grow old and die, only to be replaced by members of the next generation.  Manufacture goods, sell to customers, advance your position in the village council or church, or gain fame by traveling.  Being among the first in a given profession to die of old age makes you more memorable, earning fame points.

RATING: 6
An inverse worker placement game, where action cubes are sprinkled around the board in abundance.  Players remove cubes to trigger actions, which sometimes requires leaving a worker behind.   So there isn't a lot of blocking until later in each round.   The triangular scoring in different areas isn't particularly inspiring, and the thin and bland theme even less so.  So, it will come down to how much you like the action/cube mechanism and the time mechanism.  I like it enough to play some more, but don't feel compelled to own it.

RATING: 8.6
I like worker placement games so I figured I'd like Village. I had seen a couple of video reviews of the game before I bought/played it so I had a general idea of the gameplay, but the layers and depth of strategy that Village offers surprised me. Simple choices like which generation worker should perform which task or what means you will use to accomplish something (cubes or more time) offers a lot to think about. One of my favorite parts of the game is to look back at what your family has accomplished - were they world travelers, farmers, politicians, craftsmen, etc. Overall, a solid game that I look forward to playing more.

RATING: 6

RATING: 7
Years ago I would've quickly added Village to my collection and it would get some initial play and then sit there, and I'd wonder why. Now, I see it as a game I'd happily play if it came out at someone's house, but I've no need to own. The mechanism of taking cubes from the board in order to place your workers is clever (cube color is meaningful and limited, and black cubes push your time marker), as is the way your meeples die from the board due to the time mechanism of taking black cubes. You lose, of course, the work ability they were providing, but you hope you can send them to the victory point graveyard (legendary townsfolk) versus the unmarked graves (eh, not so much). At first blush, it's fresh and different -- what's not to like?

The play space feels too limited in how you can go about getting points, and after a few games of trying them all, it seems like I'd get bored. Feels like it needs one other dimension to take off for me. Left me with a feeling of "almost a really good game."

UPDATE: Well, I played with the Inn expansion and think that is the missing dimension for me. The bonus cards offer new ways to go in the game and much more to think about than the base game. Are they too swingy or do they require everyone to get their share such that no one player gets all the great ones? I strongly prefer playing with them to find out.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Rules Complexity: :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
Strategic Depth: :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
Luck Factor :star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Negotiation Factor: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Amount of Downtime: :star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Fiddliness: :star::halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Replayability: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Component Quality: :star::star::star::star::halfstar:
Player Interaction: :star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
"Take That" Conflict: :halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Theme Integration: :star::star::star::star::halfstar:

Village is a very solid worker placement game that gives a unique vibe with a clever implementation of the concept of dealing with the death of one's workers.  Each turn is pretty quick as the player simply picks an available cube on the board and does the corresponding action based on the location of the cube selected.  One of the cool things is that not only is the location of the cube important, but the color of the cube has meaning too, as it indicates a specific resource, or sometimes, no resource but a sickness that decreases the amount of time left for a worker to live.  However, having a short life isn't always bad - in fact killing off workers can be a part of an effective strategy, and has a direct bearing on the length of the game, and therefore the amount of time one's opponents have to perform the actions they want to do.  On the downside, there is very little player interaction, except in the sense that when I select an action, there's less of a chance that my opponents can select that action - but this is even less apparent than in a game like Agricola, since there are usually multiple chances to take the same action in Village.  It plays a little longer than I was expecting it to as well, and I'm interested to see how different each game plays from one to the next, as there doesn't seem to be as much variability as in some of my favorite games.  But I'm thrilled that I own this game, and can hardly wait to bring it out again!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A curious game. Having your workers die is an inspired idea, ripe with thematic and storytelling opportunity. Unfortunately this is saddled with a rather dry Stefan Feld-esque cube-collecting exercise, and so although the game centered around a rich thematic conceit it ultimately proves a rather abstract experience. For example, whether or not a worker gets "remembered" is nothing to do with their achivements but decided purely by the timing of their death. Similarly, we're told that the cubes we're collecting represent "Faith", "Persuasiveness" etc. but they only ever feel like green and brown cubes. That said the game is very good from a strategic perspective; just don't expect it to feel like anything. I wouldn't turn down a game, but could think of too many other games I'd rather play to hold onto this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Village borrows quite a few mechanisms from other games and combines them in new and interesting ways. The most obvious, of course, is the often commented on time track that causes your villagers to die after a while. The novelty here isn't so much that your workers can die because, let's face it, that's really just another way to "promote" them to a state where they will (hopefully) get you victory points, and you've seen that before. No, what's especially nice about it is how it puts control of the end game timing into the players' hands.

Timing is important in multiple ways, for example many of the valuable victory point spots are available in limited quantities only and the actions players can choose each turn are similarly restricted. If there is anything that detracts from the overall positive impression it's the fairly heavy upkeep requirements and that some of the paths in the game, in spite of seemingly diverging quite a bit, feel very similar in the end. This makes the game feel a bit same-y after a few plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I'm glad I got to try this out before committing to buying it. Village is actually a unique twist on the somewhat tired old worker placement games. Here you remove a cube to take an action, and then get to use that cube later on. It pretty much was standard "buy cubes and resources and convert them into other cubes and resources and then finally VPs" sort of game. However, this one was slightly marred by the fact that nothing really gelled together. They all seemed like parts of a whole that came off mashed up. "Dying" meeples is a great idea, but didn't play into the fun-factor as much as I was hoping. What was left over was a game whose theme I'm really getting tired of and whose gameplay is 80% rehash. Also, without any sort of catch-up mechanic, I pretty much knew 1 1/2 hours into it that I was tying for last place. And there was one more hour to go! In fact, the entire game was an exercise in dealing with very little. I normally like that, but here it was just tiring and grueling. Also, the game requires players to specialize in only a couple of areas. Spreading out too thin is a problem. Thus, I felt I had already explored the game thoroughly half way in and wasn't getting much excitement out of the rest of the game. Oh well, I was glad to finally try it! But I don't think I'll be playing again.

RATING: 6
Village is a particular type of Euro.  It is in the same category as "Caylus" - and they do in fact share a mechanism:  Worker Placement.  

Village innovates and expands the worker placement concept in two ways:

1. by allowing multiple activations of a particular spots (buildings) by different players, but capped at "X" number each round (X is dependent on the number of players), 

2.  by allowing you to do (or consume and deny) an action with out actually placing a worker.  Once placed, workers are "sticky" and do not return to you without sacrifice.

Village resembles the "Caylus type" in a more fundamental ways - you turn about 10 resources into other resources and ultimately victory points; the consummate "cube pusher".

One of those resources is "time" and the commitment of one of your workers to the board.  Worker placement and time can buy many things in lieu of one of the other resources.  You almost always have time and workers (either in hand or in place) and can therefore almost always take a useful action.   

In this game, workers die off (in generational order) each time you spend a dozen or so time units.

I'm not a huge fan of these types of games... so it is a "6" at best for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Very good game centered on a sort of reverse worker placement: although some actions do require a worker, you actually take a cube off the board in order to perform an action, and that is what reduces the availability of future actions in the round. Another interesting mechanic is spending time for those actions, which may cause your workers to die and eventually determines the game end. There are multiple strategies, many things to consider, and all seems pretty well balanced. The theme also works quite well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I like that there seem to multiple paths to victory, even if I don't quite get when to pick which path.  I like that unlike in many other worker placement games, there is a way to take an "unavailable" action -- use three identical cubes.

There are a lot of important decisions to make at times.  Sometimes it's a simple as looking at what other players are likely to do (especially the player to your left), and beating them to it by taking the cube or action that they want.  A good example is the market cube, which can lead to good points at no cost in time -- if you're the one to take the market action.  If you have collected goods for acquiring the same tile as another player, you want to make sure that you go first in the market day before them.

Travelling seems to be a really good deal.  The spots closest to the entrance are worth 3 points each, plus their points at the end (at least 1 point, up to 4 points).  That can make travelling one extra spot at the end worth 7 points!  Also, some spots give you cubes, which can help you get to the next spot.

The council chamber is also pretty good, especially the 6-point spot at the end, which can turn a gold into +3 VP.  It's important to remember that you only need to pay a scroll tile or 2 green cubes (and time) if you want to advance in the council; you can stay in the same spot and take that action (or a lower one) for no cost.  So I can see how wheat to gold to +3 VPs could be a good VP strategy.

It's important to remember that you get end-of-game points for *each* person that you have in the council chamber and church.  So there is a reason to put more than one person in those sections.

I'm not sure I like the game, overall... A problem is that it's a timing game.  You could be gearing up with cubes and wagon tiles for the next few actions, and then the game could suddenly end by the unmarked graves being filled up, giving you only one last action.  So you have to guess who might end the game (who's in a position and motivated to kill a person that fills up the last grave) and play based on what you guess their schedule to be.  That can lead to a lot of "if I'd only known it was going to end, I would have played differently!" comments after the game is over, which doesn't feel fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Four problems : 1)You have no push at the beginning to take a certain strategy.  You have to start building a strategy on nothing. This is why a game like Agricola has cards that you get at the start of the game. 2) It usually doesn't seem like a bad thing to take plague cubes. 3)Suffers from the same thing as Lords of Waterdeep.  It doesn't understand "worker placement". Any player can pretty much take any action at any time or they may have to just wait a little longer to to take their desired action. No blocking. 4) To continue with that point a little - there is no line of strategy that really makes sense.  I feel like you can almost just take random actions and do fine.  Too much to do with no direction to the actions. 5)  The theme.  I love the theme and how it is implemented except:  when you put workers on a craft space they make a product for you.  Why for you?  wouldn't they be making it for others and you would get an income?  Whatever.

What I do really like about this game is how much attention you have to pay  to the pace of the game set by other players.  This is a great thing about this game and I'm am usually excited to play it because of this.

RATING: 7.5
Village proves to be an interesting worker placement game which takes the time/hourglass tracking in a different direction- instead of changing turn order it measures the actual lifetimes of the meeples (sad meeples- sob!)

But though the meeples that have passed away are no longer usable for actions, they occupy spaces which (a) give victory points and (b) serve as the game timer.

In a 1st solo 4-player learning game, I found getting to the end game conditions a bit tedious (I was holding on to earlier generations too long), but with additional experience one remembers to "Race for the Graveyard," and then the endgame flows naturally.

I initially feared the idea of deceased meeples might come across oddly, but it was really not terribly different from games like Key Market, where workers are 'retired' for VP's before the end of the game.

There certainly are a number of options in play, and I can see it potentially causing a litle AP in learning games, but overall I think this game flows well and would not cause too much thought for expeienced gamers.

I am starting this at a 7, with real climbing potential if I find (as I expect to) that it flows to a satisfying conclusion much better with plays under one's belt. 

Updates: Bumped up to a 7.25 after 2nd game (3-player solo); after fixing the cube distribution rule and the timing for interment of meeples I found this played in the expected time period, and I liked the flow much better. 

Bumped up to a 7.5 after 3rd game (2-player solo).  I was really pleasantly surprised how well the simple 2-player adjustments retained the taut feeling of the available actions, and kept the game-length down. This is a solid game at all player counts so far, and was well-worth the impulsive pick-up.  Now to see if the family like it!

RATING: 7
(05/09/2014) Comment based on 1 play.

This is an interesting action-drafting, pseudo-worker placement game, that follows the lives of multiple generations in some country village. Each player has a number of family members that can be categorized into four generations.

On the board there are a number of action spaces, which are tied to specific buildings or events. At the beginning of each turn, a seemingly random assortment of cubes are placed on each location. A player will draft an action associated with the building or event, by selecting a cube from the corresponding spot. Once cubes are gone from that spot, the action can no longer be drafted. Some spots have more cubes than others. Thus, players must balance near-term objectives versus long-term ones based on the actions and the specific color of the cube they are pulling.

This is because most of the action spaces require expenditure of specific combinations of colored cubes that were collected beforehand, in order to make progress in a particular building or event.

The action spaces are:
1) Cart --> collect food.
2) Travel --> expend specific colored cube combinations a wagon, and time, to simulate the travel experiences of one of your family members.
3) Market --> expend hardware, scrolls, food, or some combination, in order to garner VP.
4) Crafthouses --> expend either specific colored cube combinations or time to get hardware, scrolls, or money.
5) Church --> expend a cube or time, and enroll one of your family members to become a friar in the church.
6) Church --> Get married and have a child, which brings out a worker from the 2nd, 3rd, and 4th generations.
7) Council House --> expend reputation cubes and time, in order to gain political power (and first turn).

As noted, some of these actions require "time", which is kept track.

Initial rating: "7".

(01/01/2015) Update after 2nd play.

I understand and am more comfortable with the action-drafting/cube-pulling mechanism, as well as the time progression mechanism this time around than the first. Although I enjoyed myself more and eventually won the game, this game left me feeling flat. As the time progresses around on each player's tableau, family members die off and are laid to rest in the "Chronicles", where more members means more bonus points. The game flows quickly if players know what they are doing, pulling cubes and drafting actions, but it is really not that all exciting. In terms of theme, there really is no rhyme or reason for the seeding and pulling of specific colored cubes in any of the action spaces, and this part feels disconnected and abstract to me (much like the mancala mechanism in Trajan, but for some reason I don't mind it in that game). The only tension is wondering if a critical action that is important to you is still there, it is a matter of prioritizing the action-drafting, as well as "timing" the aging of your family members so they are laid to rest quickly to occupy a space in the Chronicle. It is still a lukewarm, meh "7". Good game with interesting mechanics, but not all too exciting.

RATING: 7
The best Euros are based around a great theme and that applies here big time. The rose-tinted image of living, marrying and dying in a small community is very well captured and sets up a great gameplay experience. This is really a worker placer with a twist, instead of sending workers to capture spaces, you take a resource cube from a space until all have been taken. Very much a game that works best at small scale (2-3 Players), there's more chaos with 4 and I'm rather glad the designers didn't allow for 5 which they easily could have. It's very much an exercise in action efficiency and with tight scoring, random elements like who is drawn from the church bag can effect end of game placements. Above average for a recent Euro.

RATING: 9
Village is a limited action allowance game by worker placement mechanic. The game describes a daily life and livelihood of generations in the medieval times. Most of all, the game has successuflly expressed people's routine works in the middle ages. Unlike other worker placement games, in Village, workers are vanished with the passage of time. Because of the time variable, players have to consider the arrangement of workers. With these new mechanical challenges, players are faced with more tactical considerations. Village provieds lots of tactical choices to players. Moreover, Village gives a good lesson for the game participants about the transient life.

RATING: 10
Of all the games I've played of the "Euro" persuasion, this one is my favorite. Unlike a lot of the prototypical Eurogames, I feel the theme in Village really fits the mechanics and breathes additional life and narrative into the game. In addition to that, the game itself is very, very solid. I love the various routes to victory, and how all of the resources in the game interact with one another on some level. It makes for a very organic and satisfying experience in trying to optimize one's available resources and options. 

I also really love the subtle black humor of the game. The game is essentially about having your family toil away their existence in their chosen field, working themselves to death in the eponymous medieval village. The ultimate catch being the only ones that really matter are those that are remembered for their work by the rest of the village. Maybe I read too much into it, but I love the sort of cynical reflection the game presents about our own culture and the ways we attribute meaning to our lives, while at the same time being so darned charming and pleasant about it. At first I thought the cutesy art style clashed with the rather morbid theme and mechanics, but now I've grown to think it's perfect. It sort of gives me a Monty Python vibe.

One of my top games. I love it!

RATING: 7
While Village caught many a gamer's attention with the pitch that their families members can die to score points, that's not really what this game is all about. It's really more a typical Euro where points are scored in many ways combined with almost the opposite of worker placement (players remove tokens rather than placing them to take actions). The use of time and death is novel, but this is a pretty standard Euro game. I enjoy those, so I've kept this game in my collection. Others may not be so kind.

For those who do like this game: get the Village Inn expansion. It improves the already solid base game.

RATING: N/A
I've only played once, but I'm impressed so far. I like the time/"death of a family member" mechanic especially. Your "time" often seems like something you need to use wisely, but at other times you want to waste it and kill off family members for those sweet, sweet VPs.

This is also probably the most thematically interesting "medieval town" game I've played. The permanent placement of workers makes each one seem unique and special. I still feel sorry for Novice Gerald, one of my first generation family members, who spent almost the entire game trying to get promoted in the church, but kept getting passed over for more worthy recruits, and those whose families could offer a bribe. He finally got his first promotion, but died off soon thereafter.

RATING: 8
The game continues to grow on me. Having now seen people pursuing widely different strategies in order to score points, all those strategies seem viable: The church, the town hall, and the market are all obvious candidates for many end-of-game points. Travel might be too, but it seems awfully expensive, and you need to commit to it wholeheartedly in order to score big points.

Getting your workers into the chronicle is important too, but (for us at least) it hasn't been a mindless rush to get them there: While true that one shouldn't necessarily see the death of a meeple as a bad thing, it really depends on timing, and just mindlessly killing everyone off as soon as possible seems like a bad strategy.

So far, at least, Village continues to impress.

------
A positive surprise: While the game doesn't seem particularly well-polished (or necessarily balanced, I don't know), it's a very thematic euro and for a worker-placement game, it seems very *different* somehow (and not just the 'death' mechanism). A breath of fresh air in a somewhat stagnant sub-genre...

RATING: 7
2012-10-03

Initial Rating: 7.0 (September 2012)

I was pleasantly surprised by Village. I guess I DO like eurogames :D. Village doesn’t really bring anything really new to the table (except for the dying meeples, that´s a first for me) but what it brings it does well. It is a lot more meaty than I initially thought, a big plus for me. The game has a lot of luck (The mass and the seeding of the cubes each round) but it can be managed, so it really doesn’t bothers me. The player who best plan his strategy will be likely the player who wins, that is, I feel the luck, although high, doesn’t impact negatively the game.  
 
I particularly like the meeples dying mechanic, you have to plan very well how quickly and from where you will “kill” your family members :devil: and the fact that you have to kill them in generation order adds another layer of strategy to the game. Sometimes you need to kill them fast and other times you need them to live a little longer. I love that.

The game turns flow easily, I didn’t feel any downtime with this game.

The components are very well done and the art is adequate although a little dull for my taste. I don’t like the theme (as with most Euros) but the gameplay is so good I can ignore that. The game board serves well its purpose, it is easy to understand and explain the game to new players as everything is so clearly depicted. 

Bottom line it is a solid game. I definitively want to play again, for now this is in my “like to have” list of games but I think it can get an upgraded rating.

RATING: 9
Solidly designed worker placement game. The death mechanic is a new and creative idea. The cube placement mechanic is also quite smart, it adds a level of randomness to the action spot seeding, and also acts as a currency to fuel other actions. Adding in the Inn and Port expansions just improve the game, add more choices and improve some of the less used mechanics of the base game (i.e. travel). While the game is quite thematic, the theme itself is a bit dry (i.e. farm family living near a village). However the expansions really spice up the theme and bring this already solid game up to a 9. Adding the pub patrons and the sailing mechanic adds nicely to the theme and makes the game less dry and more adventurous.

RATING: 9
June 1 Update:  Upgraded to a 9 after about two dozen plays.  This has been the most played game in my collection for 2012, just about everyone I have shown it to has enjoyed it, many have come back for repeated plays.  No two games have played exactly the same, and there appear to be multiple paths to victory.  Play balance has held up, so this is my candidate for Game of the Year -- note it is a finalist for the KdJ (Kennerspiel) award.

Tentative rating based after five plays.  Have played a number of the Essen 2011 releases and, right now, this would have to be my candidate for the best of the bunch.  Rules are fairly straightforward, there are multiple paths to victory, and the rather unique element where certain workers that are placed "die" during the course of the game adds an element that I have not seen in other games.  Hopefully, the game will not get stale with multiple plays but, as of this writing (Feb. 2012), it's my favorite of the fall 2011 releases.

RATING: 10
One of the best (if not the best) Eurogames I've ever played.
Great theme and feel of the game, with a great deal of strategy.

This is a worker placement game with a twist, eventually your workers (or "family members" as they are called in the rules) will die, which at first made me little sad when I lost some of my workers early in the game. But late in the game (a bit too late in fact) I realized that it can be desireable to have your workers die off early in the game to get them into the village chronicle before the other players (a bit morbid I know, but there is lots of easy points to be obtained that way).

RATING: 7
Village is a worker placement / action selection game that convinces by solid gameplay coupled with the extraordinary setting of everyday life in a medieval city, i.e. you are not building a cathedral but instead your folks are working, praying and dying. Especially the time mechanism for dying is unique enough to justify yet another worker placement game. Beside that, there are various ways to victory, the game end can be timed by the deaths of workers and there are many small races to be ahead one step of others to make Village stand out from the masses. Rating might increase with more plays.

RATING: 6
Another worker placement game with the unique aspect of time and workers dying.  It kept things interesting but ultimately I didn't feel like my choices were that difficult.  I prefer games with heavier theme as well.  It was an average game but there are so many other games I prefer.  
A MAJOR complaint with the game after 3 plays is the seemingly single path to victory.  There is little to no benefit of spending time and resources to travel or go to the guilds much or really in killing off your meeples to the book.  The game is won and lost in the city hall, church, and market.  The same strategy has won all 3 games I have played so far with the player(s) going for travel finishing a DISTANT 3rd and 4th. 
There is also minimal variability in gameplay from game to game.  The only inherent difference is they way the cubes, meeples, and market buyers are pulled from the bag.  That's it.  The game feels the same every time.  Too much randomness is bad but so is near complete lack of randomness.

RATING: 5
Good but limited game. I find the economy more tight than fun. There are four or five resources you can produce and you spend the game trying to produce as many of them as you can. You hustle through workers to try to score points via good timing on top of the action options. It's tough to math out, a plus, but the path to victory is be lucky and opportune. 

Games like this are why I love Agricola, which requires both a strategy and tactics. This is only the latter and not much of the former. Too much changes in a 4p from round to round to make planning worthwhile. You take what the other players give you and it may or may not synch with your plans. 

It's well designed but not that much fun for me.

RATING: 7.5
05-05-2014 - After one play... I'm not sure yet.  Well, I know I like it, I'm just not sure how much.  This is a pretty typical cube pusher - you need cubes, they're hard to get, it's hard to do everything you want to do, etc.  But the killing, er, "retiring" of family members really does make this game very, well... pretty different.  It adds a whole new kind of planning requirement to the typical worker placement game.  Tough decisions at times.  The game definitely does not "play itself" either, you are in control and have to make something out of nothing from the get-go.  7.5 for now, I could see it getting as high as 8.5, but that's probably the ceiling without an expansion to make it more interesting (and this game seems like it would be pretty easy to expand).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
This is an extremely convoluted game that I consider to be unplayable.

The new idea that this game introduce is the fact that people age and that previous generations can leave a trace in history. It they would have focused on that idea only they could have made a great game. I would even added the ability for ancestors to give power ups to newer generations like if some ancestral power were transferred from a generation. But unfortunately, village does no have such cool feature.

Village is not only a worker placement game, it's a double worker placement game. You take actions by picking up cubes and sometimes it requires a worker. So you must plan cube pickup and worker placement.

It also the kind of games where there are many options possible that almost does the same thing, so you could get what you want in one way or another. But the problem is that the resource flow is so complex, if there is one, that if you try to draw a graph of it, you are going to end up with a spider web.

It's also the kind of game where what ever you do, every player is going to end with approximately with the same number of VP. So all path lead to victory and there is not really a way to get ahead or behind.

The worst part is that some actions, power ups or other things are absolutely unbalanced. Some actions have very expensive cost for no good reason (for example the merchant). 

You also have these buildings where you could upgrade your character by making them raise rank. It's a cool idea, but there is no reason to raise older generation in ranks because they are going to die for sure, and there is no reward for dying in a higher rank, you only score at the end of the turn. So you just better leave it rank 1, make sure it dies to send it as fast as you can in the chronicle.So this is another reason I think that the game is seriously broken. 

Finally, something much illogical with the theme, families age differently according to what they do. When has it happened in history that some people ages slower than other. Please tell me, I want to know if I could save some years. Instead of placing a time cost on some actions, they should have made all people age at the same time and give a unique number of each pawn.

There was a game of 1856 running while we played, and I thought that this game was actually easier to play than village. At least, you know where the money comes from and where it get spent.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Definitely an interesting worker placement type of game where you must balance death of your meeples with maximizing output and VPs.  Different strategies for victory seem to be present.  If you can pursue the strategy less chosen by others, you will likely win.  This is a solid, middleweight game.

RATING: 6
So I hate Worker Placement games, always have, and most likely always will. WP games are the reason I started turning away from Euro games in general, which I guess I should be thanking the WP lot for in some strange way. That boring strange little man on the cover of Caylus, he can go right off and fuck himself for all I care.

That being said, Village is probably the best WP game I have played to date (and yes i have played more than just Caylus). While listening to the explanation of the rules I couldn't but think that it would be the same thing every other worker placement game was, and would bore the shit out of me for 3 hours. Well I was in for a shock, it was only 90 min, big plus in my book. The mechanics are definitely not very thematic, but being thematic is not a requisite for being a WP game. Village also turns the usually somewhat competitive aspect of WP games, really into something a little bit more cuthrroat. 

The generations mechanic is definitely the backbone of the game, and makes for very interesting choices throughout the game. This was definitely a good choice for the structure of this game as it is clearly the most interesting of all of the mechanics in the game. 

The theme is really quite terrible, and the graphic design is abhorant, but overall if I had to play a WP game this would be it at this point. 

Hey Caylus dude, I thought I told you go to fuck yourself?

RATING: 8
While I cannot think of any specific examples at the time I write this, Village is one of a newer breed of games that offers a fair variety of scoring possibilities, with a fair number of routes to obtain each, the trick is that all of them are equal opportunity. What's more is that because all things are fairly equal and it's by the narrow margin of points here or there that you will win.

You have to keep track of the big picture, not only that but the fact that you have to time the use of your workers as they age out in order, this latter bit being the most interesting and tension inducing. I quite enjoyed what this package had to offer. It does fill a niche in my collection, which means that it will see more play and stick around for some time to come.

RATING: 6
I keep comparing [thing=104006][/thing] to [thing=102680][/thing] because the both are point salad style games; [thing=104006][/thing] is a bit more interactive as there are only so many actions of each type up for grabs.  Along with the decision over which action to take, there's the added decision of which colour cube to take with it.  As the cube colours influence what you can do with your actions, you may find that you're competing for the pinks or the greens with another player.  In [thing=102680][/thing], your action is selected through your movement of cylinders around your own mancala.  It's done in isolation - although your action may result in you picking up a counter or a card that your opponent may have wanted.  But given the options available in [thing=102680][/thing], your taking what someone else wanted is rarely a ball-crushing move.  In [thing=104006][/thing], late in a round, it can be.  There are times when the only cubes left are black (plague) and the action they are linked to gives you no benefit at all.

RATING: N/A
Definitely a euro.
TV Doesn't particularly like it; despite gorgeous art etc.

Ender says:
So, is Village a game for you? If you approach Village with too high expectations as an award winning, innovative, and richly thematic game, you do run the risk of being disappointed, and even judging the game unfairly. But if you approach Village as a quintessential cube-pushing euro, you will really appreciate what new mechanics it introduces and what theme is present. It may not be a ground-breaking game set to alter the direction and design of modern games, but it does offer a fresh approach to a well worn genre, and it's certainly a splendid example of an attractive and thematic euro done well. As such, Village should deservedly go down in the history books as a worthy winner of the Kennerspiel award. This is an outstanding euro, that rightly comes highly recommended, and which fans of euro games should not miss.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The theme in this game is brilliantly executed, and though somewhat generic, it has enough charm to be engaging. Top quality components and gorgeous artwork make this a very appealing game.

Worker placement games are a dime a dozen these days, but this is one of the few that does something unique, in the form of workers that die of old age. This creates some interesting strategic dilemmas. Investing effort in end-game scoring buildings early (such as the Church and Town Hall) is difficult to do, as the workers may well die before the end of the game, but it also takes time to advance workers in these buildings. There is also an advantage to be gained by having workers from certain professions die early, as the spaces in the village chronicle fill up quickly.
