RATING: 8
Rather original worker placement game with nice looking material. Theme feels very present.

What is special about this game is that your workers come in "generations". You have to be real careful which generations to put on the board because the older ones die earlier. This adds a new dimension to gaming (aging workers).

The game takes a bit long and there is a lot of setup work to do, also during the game, after each turn. What is missing is game tension. Probably because it's not clear during the game who is winning. 
Also the game lacks variation. What makes it kind of boring after a number of plays.

Minus: 3 (duration) - [-1]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Maybe I’m jaded, maybe Village and I got off to a bad start, but after one play this seems like the quintessential soulless cubepusher that all those people must be talking about when they dismiss any game without dice. A couple of methods to garner victory points, a couple of completely interchangable resources that, unsurprisingly, get interchanged a lot, and exactly one new twist on the same-old, same-old in the mix.

The worst thing is that all of this comforting lack of originality did not at least add up to any challenging decisions in my first and probably only game– we each formed some kind of plan, made our moves roughly according to that plan, and ended up with extremely close results in spite of one experienced player against two newbies and wildly different approaches.

Awards and a lot of positive comments suggest that I’m missing something, here, but even if there is a lot more to Village than met my eye, I’m still bewildered why anyone would play this instead of classic Euros like Caylus or Puerto Rico, where both the tension and the skill factor are miles beyond what is on offer here.

RATING: 10
I am so impressed with this game! Excellent scalability. 

Update 7/20/14: Played this game with 4 today. Previously, we had only played this with 2, and it is EXCELLENT with 2. Of course, it's even better with 4. What a great game. We played with 2 muggles and the Village totally clicked for both of them by the end of the first round, maybe before. On the surface, this is not a gateway game, as its weight and variety of options can be quite daunting for a non-gamer. Interestingly though, it's that same variety that offers new players a good deal of options for strategic planning. You can literally just pick any part of the game, exploit it to the best of your ability, and still feel like your doing well. Plus, the game is not that "tight," even with 4. I felt like I got to do pretty much whatever I wanted. This looseness, if you will, opens the game up and eases the stress for non-gamers. Fantastic game. Can't say enough about how good it is. I never thought it could be a gateway, but I was wrong!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Village is your typical, run-of-the-mill, euro-style, worker placement game...except it isn't.  There is one substantial difference that elevates this above most other worker placements.  The difference is that there are multi-generational families in this game.  The family you control will have members born, live out their lives, and die over the course of play.  It's a simple twist on the typical mechanism within worker placements of getting more workers.  However, that twist allows Village to tell a compelling narrative of the life and times of the family you control.

Other than that it's standard stuff.  You put your workers out to gather resources, or complete other tasks that will garner points.  Whomever has the most points wins.

The components are very nice.  The board is a beautifully drawn rendition of a village complete with various buildings, roads, and people that your family will navigate through, and interact with.

Worker placement games can be boring affairs at times.  If you were to try just one, you would be making an excellent choice by visiting the good people of Village.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Location: 2-3-1R
I also have the following expansions:
* customer expansion
* Village Inn

After our first game, I'm just floored thinking about how elegant this Euro game is.  After first play, I can see not touching the manual except for remembering end-game scoring.

Looks like plenty of ways to score VPs.  Not only do randomly drawn color cubes determine how many times an action can be done, but actual color you pick has an effect on what you can do next, which also affects subsequent actions.

Well done components and functional board art.  Fun original theme and narrative during game.  Seems to play well at 2, and I'm guessing will be just as fun (if not more for contention for Mass) with more folks.  First game a 3 hours (which seems long), but see it going by faster with subsequent plays.

I've played quite a few Euros, and if I were to start rating, this would be up there.

RATING: 6
09/08/12 Played a three-player game. The game's main difference from other Eurogame worker placement "cube pushers" is one's people die off after a time. This is rare, but its not unique (In the Shadow of the Emperor comes to mind). However the rest of the game is pretty much a standard worker placement cube pusher with multiple (7) paths to gaining victory points. So much so that it pretty much didn't matter what you did, you'd make almost the same amount of points as the other players. Overall I found Village a pleasant diversion that I could play a few times. Though nothing about it truly grabs me. In a word its inoffensive; not exaclty a ringing endorsement is it?  The artwork was nice, but nothing special (to me, after playing and rating over 550 games). I will say this, everything was logical and everything had a name. Example, each cube color actually represented something. That's more than I can say for some recent games I've played like Macao's  unnamed "action cubes" and Trajan's unnamed action cylinders (both these games are by the same designer of Village, Stefen Feld). I'll add there isn't much player interaction unless you consider picking up a certain cube before someone else does.

RATING: 7
[microbadge=1714]

[u]21-May-2013[/u]:  Village is a solid entry in the classic, cube-pushing, Eurogame genre.  You collect resources (Influence cubes), which allows you to take various actions, and in turn, earn victory points for end game scoring.  The one twist is that your family members, will pass away and (hopefully) be remembered in the Village Chronicle, the book of remembrance of those extra special villagers left their mark on society...and earn you victory points.

My few plays (two solo, one 2P) so far were very enjoyable.  My concern is that you may fall into a pattern rather easily in terms of where you want to earn your VP.  There are five main ways, I believe.  So you'll most likely do really well in two of them and hope your smaller VP areas are enough to beat out your opponents'.

Either way, if you enjoy a good Eurogame, I would definitely recommend you give this one a look.

[u]26-Jan-2014[/u]:  I've played this several more times now as the family took to it in the second half of 2013.  It's a well produced and solid entry in the Euro game arena.  It's all about multiple paths to victory and resource management.  The unique "dying" mechanism helps this one stand out amongst other, similar action selection games.  I feel the theme does show through the gameplay and is well worth adding to any collection.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
We really like this one. Just needs a bit more variety.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Action selection game where players trigger actions at various stops in the village by taking cubes from the board. The cubes represent the various resources needed to purchase goods (carts, etc.) and pay for services within the village. 

There are a ton of ways to score victory points and players need to play both a tactical and strategic game to assure victory. The meat of the game is that actions are limited each round and timing is essential. Oh, speaking of time. Actions take time, the time you spend on actions age your workers. Yes, they die and you can score points if you time their deaths too. Of course there's an action to spawn future generations.

RATING: 8
This is one of those games you hear so much hype about and worry if it is driven purely by its novelty. Your workers die? How inventive! But that's underselling it. I find the worker placement label for this game a misrepresentation. Your action selection is really driven by taking cubes which are various types of currency or punishment. Players may select an action they don't care about just for the currency or may grab a useless currency just for the action or may be blocking their opponents from either the action or the currency or both. However the most important currency may be time, which is novel not so much because it is "new" but because of how it is used--roughly speaking you start with an infinite amount of it and it is sometimes your most precious commodity and sometimes deadweight to be jettisoned. At times you are lamenting the untimely death of your wainwright before you made that final wagon and other times you can't kill off your farmer fast enough to solidify his place in the annals of farming celebrity. Timing these things to maximize their usefulness and then reassigning them, whether temporarily or permanently, is at the heart of the strategy. In fact the curse cubes, the aforementioned "punishments," are themselves of variable value throughout the game depending which way you want to push time. It is the intertwining of different *types* (not colors or labels or uses) of resources that differ in the very way they exist and transform that makes this game fascinating. I don't know if it will have infinite replayability, as the built-in variance is quite small and the currency distinctions themselves may be the novelty that wears off, but I want to play this one a lot more.

RATING: 8
Really enjoyable worker placement game that offers up a satisfying combination of short an long term scoring systems. 

What sets it apart from rival worker placement titles is the aging mechanism whereby taking various actions costs time and when your time marker rounds your board, one of your villagers dies and you must always kill off one of the oldest villagers that remain. 

This may sound like a bad thing but dying can allow a family to enter the Chronicle, which simulates a villager achieving high status in one area of the village. 

All in all it is an engaging and varied experience that falls in the 50-80 minute time frame.

RATING: 9
A worker placement game with a twist. There are lots of different strategies, and what I like most is it doesn't particularly reward or penalize you to diversify/concentrate on certain actions. The cutthroat-ness of the game is due to everything being so scarce - action selection, meeples, time and cubes, however some of them can be expended depends on your style of play. The right mix of tightness and rooms for creative play is what makes it one of the better Euro that came out recently.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
In a village, players choose actions between increasing family members, putting family members in the church, craft, council chambers or travelling. The unique mechanism in this game is that as time increases, your family members die off - some can earn you VPs if they make it to the right graveyard, otherwise, they score nothing in unmarked graves!

The mechanic in this game is that a player selects a colored cube in one of the areas in the village to execute the actions for that area. Different colored cubes are required for many actions, and at anytime, a  player can trade in 3 cubes of the same kind to execute an action at a location.

There are many ways to earn VPs, either by selling goods, or putting family members into the church. There are lots of different strategies to pursue and different approaches to the game. This is a good game, but I do have a couple criticisms: There is no balancing off for player order, so if you are playing a 4 player game, and there are 4 cubes in the birthing action, and one of them is black, the last player will take a black hit with no compensation. Maybe this isn't all bad, allowing you to get to the village chronicles first, but it is disadvantage as the other players are up one cube on you. Secondly, the game can end quite suddenly and you have to make sure you don't get caught with many cubes in your hand.

RATING: 7
I had reservations about Village from what I'd seen of it on Shut Up & Sit Down. They liked it, but it just seemed to be another medieval-themed, worker placement, point salad. Well, nothing beats having low expectations proven wrong and that is what Village did. Yes, it was the medieval-themed, point salad I expected, but the generational nature of one's workers made for additional strategy in their placement. In this way it reminded me a bit of Small World.

A few strange things about Village are that one never needs to actually place one's workers, and no locations on the board are ever denied to another player's workers. Because of this, I don't really feel Village is a Worker Placement game as those types of games have an element of denying your opponents access to certain actions by getting there first, but in Village all actions are always available.

I'm not looking to add Village to my collection though. It is just too hard to explain to new players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
While reading reviews and the rules I had in my head how this game would play being a worker placement and all.  It didn't.  More so than any other game (or close to it) this game plays MUCH differently than how I imagined it in my head.  That is not because of the death mechanic, although that is a large part, it is more about how actions are selected.  Taking away a cube to take an action is different and a bit hard to put your finger on until after playing.

If you describe this game as just another worker placement game, you are giving it a disservice, just for the fact that it is more accurately described as an action selection/worker placement.  

The added planning of deciding which family member to place and when to kill them off adds to the intrigue.  A solid game 'in that genre', but quite different than many you have played before.

A solid '8' after a 4 and 2 player game.

RATING: 8.5
Worker placement game that simulate the different generations of some families living in a village. It made me remember Gabriel García Márquez's "100 años de soledad" :D. Your workers actually die, but you can get sons, great-sons and great-great-sons.

The game offers you many choices and from what I have tested, many different paths for victory. Up to now, I have found it perfectly balanced.

Even if at its core it is a worker placement game where you move cubes, transform them, and so on, if you come to think it, the theme is very well integrated (the different families, the different cubes each meaning some skill or quality that allow you do different things depending on that skill/quality, etc).

And it's not a long game, you can really play it in 70-90 minutes.

For me, it's a game with many "likes" and no "dislikes" :)

RATING: 10
This game is a "7" without the Port expansion. I give it a 10 with Port. For me it's not an expansion, Port is a PATCH :) You may or may not play it with the first expansion (Inn), cause it adds some flavour to the game, but the second expansion (Port) kind of FIXES the travelling part of the game. You score while travelling AND sell stuff you take with you to the lands you travel to AND you later sell in the Market stuff you brought from your travellings. AND you bring back treasure chests with money. It is a totally different game, do not play it without the Port expansion, buy the Inn if you really really like this game like me, but do not try this game without Port if you can afford it.

RATING: 8.5
規則:
*每個人拿對應顏色圖版工人跟圓片，起始四個等級一的米寶，一個圓片放分數，一個放在個人圖版記錄時間，每個人起始一元
*主圖版根據人數開商人版塊，戒指放在議事廳第一格，最年長的成為起始玩家
*每輪開始時先跟據設定卡放對應數量方塊進袋中並在每個行動抽出對應數量方塊
*每人輪流拿一個方塊並執行動作，拿黑色就失去兩個時間(也可拿了不做)，全部拿完此輪結束進行彌薩
1.拿麥 根據手上有的版塊拿麥，個人圖版上限存五個，一定要有人才能做
2.家人 拿目前數字最小的工人放在家園圖版(也可改成拿場上的回家園版塊)
3.鑄造 可花時間放人去上面拿物品，或是直接用方塊換，人會帶在對應建築物上
4.市場 啟動的人可支付物品拿走商人版塊記分，之後輪流可支付時間跟方塊來交易，直到所有人pass，把下面的按順序補到上面空位，在開新的
5.旅行 支付方塊跟篷車時間在旅行區旅行，旅行到的位置拿資源，地點放圓片，遊戲最後記分
6.議事廳 支付時間跟方塊或書卷放人上去或是移動人往上，可向下拿資源，或是不花時間跟資源直接執行此格或以下的特權
7.教會 加一個人到袋中
8.井 不用拿方塊改成付三個同色方塊，可坐上面7的動作任選
*該輪結束後直行望彌薩 先輪流看要不要付一塊錢直接出來(最多四個人出來)，如果不到四個在抽到四個為止，黑色的直接丟回袋中>在來付麥錢進，照順位，可前進多格或多個前進一格 > 人數最多的加兩分，平手看誰最前面，在平手就都加兩分
*錢可代替任何顏色方塊，最後一個一分
*行動後過橋，在該行動執行完在掛人，市場日則必須立刻掛，掛人一定要從數字小的挑，根據掛的人工作的地方去名譽榜，滿人就去無名塚
*當名譽榜躺滿人或是無名塚躺滿人當輪結束，達成條件以外的人都還有最後一回合，如果場上沒方塊，則可選擇任意行動
*如果是在市集日結束且觸發條件的不是執行市集日的人，則全部玩家有最後一回合
*最後一輪結束有最後一次望彌薩
*分數  旅行 議事廳 教會 名譽榜 商人版塊(分數此時才記錄) 金幣 平手比商人版塊在平手比活著的人數目

農村出代誌
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
One of those Euros where you can admire the breadth of the design and the work which must have gone into it to make it balanced and interesting, and the gradual killing off of meeples due to the build-up of time-cost of doing actions is kind of interesting, but it's ultimately an exercise rather than fun. An exercise in developing a base strategy in one of the key ways to score points (markets, travel, church, etc), concentrating on investing in the tools needed to execute that strategy well, and then grinding it out until enough meeples have died to trigger the end of the game at which point you add everything up. There's enough replay in the game to allow you to explore the different facets ... if that's the kind of challenge you're after. At least it's an organically satisfying ending (enough deaths) rather than the rote set number of rounds that too many games rely on.

RATING: N/A
Sold.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
"Solid action-drafting game that requires careful planning. A bit static without the expansion."

What I like:
- Solid "action drafting" (nee worker placement) game, with multiple paths to victory
- Some blocking, and minor majority aspect, but no attacks
- The theme holds the game together quite well

What I don't like:
- Cubes detract from the theme
- Some of the board iconology could be clearer
- I slightly prefer games with a fixed end, rather than allowing players to speed/slow the game
- The static board may hurt replayability eventually
- 2p might get quite cutthroat (not sure yet)

It's a good game. I'm having trouble loving it, but I'm also having trouble coming up with complaints about the game. It's too complex for non-gamers. Originally I thought the weight was 2.9 (below the BGG 3.1), but after watching gamers try to learn it from the book, I think 3.2 is a better guess (due to complexity more than depth). 

Our 4p learning game was a bit long (2 hrs), but a 2p game took just under 60. One 4p game with 2 first-timers took 2.25 hours after .5 hours of rules, which is really too long.

Rating is a high 7 without the expansion; low 8 with.

RATING: 7.5

A nice Euro where the path to victory is not evident.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
For all the hype this game received, I was a little underwhelmed.  There are two basic ideas going on here: converting your cubes into points, and racing to get your dead guys into the book.  People tend to view "wasting time" as a bad thing, but it's not, because you want to kill off your guys as quickly as possible.  What's interesting is balancing the two ideas: you have to make sure the guys you're killing off aren't the guys getting you most of your points.  Once you understand that, the game is fairly straightforward.  A little bland, even.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 5
Traded away at Alphaspel.

***

Several years have past since I played it for the first and only time.. and I have no real desire playing it again. I have looked into the rulebook a couple of times after that first play, trying to pick it up again, but I just get bored. I usually like to read rules

I don't know, there is something that is really off-putting & convoluted about this game. 

***

Just one 4-player yet..

This game actually adds some context to your usually anonymous meeples, and that's nice. Good integrated theme. 

Interesting mechanics this "cube picking" to select an action. Of course, the cubes availible for your desired action - if there are any left - is not necessarily one that might come in handy for _that_ particular action. So you often do many actions to be able too realize some other actions later on. 

My concern is that the rounds seems like too long before all the cubes have been picked up. Sometimes it feels like you run out of interesting options before it is time to hold a mass prior to a new round.

Anyway, aside from the complaint above this game gave a pretty good first impression.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Rock solid cube pusher, of the "there are x different areas to score points, so figure out where you want to focus and where you want to dabble"-variety.

Interesting action draft system where you pull cubes from the board to perform an action, so you must weigh the type (color) of a cube vs. the connected action, and guesstimate how likely it is that there will still be available cubes - and thus, actions - next turn if you skip it and give the other players a chance to take it.

There are enough paths to victory that getting beaten to the punch or screwed out of a spot barely hurts; plan B is almost as good.

Village feels like one of the better medium-weight "collect cubes and meeples and chits and trade them for points in a dozen different ways"-games out there; that said, there are a million similar games, and Village doesn't bring enough to rise above the other ones.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fun game where actions are taken by removing a resource from an action area and then adding it to your stockpile. The different areas get seeded with new resources each round. In addition, some actions need you to send a worker to go stand in a spot, and some actions require you to spend "time" which is a personal resource you have on the front of your board. When the time counter makes a full rotation, your lowest numbered worker dies. If he dies doing the right thing in the right spot, he'll be worth endgame points to you. If not, he'll just advance the endgame faster.

The game is similar to Feld games in that every area of the board is it's own little mechanic and each gives points. The mechanics can feel a little cumbersome sometimes, because from early on in the game you want to both get the most out of your pawns, but you also need to kill them fast enough to ensure they are worth something later on. It's an interesting tension.

:star: Art and components
:nostar: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6

If it's a low interaction type of game, it should at least give me a headache. "Village" did not.

RATING: 8
The graveyard/time mechanic is wonderfully innovative for worker placement games. Players should be very familiar with the end game scoring conditions before playing this one, as just about everything plays a part and it's a very tight race to the finish. 

The plague cubes are an interesting twist too. Originally I thought they were something to be avoided and later discovered their strategic value of rushing the end game and getting meeples into the chronicle quicker than your opponents.

Overall, Village is a very neat design with mechanics that flow elegantly throughout. I can only see this rating going up with future plays.

RATING: 8.61
Here's a video play-through and review:  http://www.boardgamegeek.com/video/42073/village/the-charters-village-play-through-and-review

Fun worker placement game.  Twist is that your 11 workers are from 4 generations and as you take actions that take time, your older workers will die off.  The timing of when/how you kill off your older workers is a strategic and tactical element of this game.

Rather than place your meeples to select actions, each round the board is seeded with resource cubes, and to take actions you pick up a cube in that area of the board.  So you collect resources in the same mechanism that you select your actions.  Very unique mechanic!   Sometimes  you really want the resource but don't care about the action.   other times you really want to take a particular action, but you don't care about the resource that you will get...the resource (plague cube) can sometimes hurt you.

Good game.  Recommended.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In my view Village is the epitomy of a cube pusher Euro game. There is a pasted on theme of doing different things in a medieval village, but all mechanisms feel totally gamey and seem to be there for their own sake. Actually, village is my go-to example for having a complete disconnect between game mechanisms and theme (aka "pasted on theme"). 

You gather ressources automatically by actually doing completely other things (I am not even sure if the ressources have names apart from green/brown/orange/rose); to get villagers into the church they have to be (more or less) randomly drawn from a bag; you can only get children, if there are ressources left on the corresponding action area.

At least the time mechanism and letting your villagers die (such that they are remembered in the village's chronicles) is quite neat, but still very gamey. This is for sure a well designed modern Euro game, but with its victory point salad and totally pasted on mechanisms it does not hit the right spot with me.

RATING: 7
The main draw for me to this game is not with it's mechanics, but in its theme. If you're looking for a good worker placement, there are better options other places. If you're looking to use your imagination, there's nothing quite like this. Pulling the drunkard in the family back to the farm before he ends up drinking himself to death at the tavern, and then having that family member die a peaceful death back on the farm is one of the most thematic moves in gaming.

The main problem with this game is it doesn't do enough to change things up from play to play. Village Inn certainly helps, though. I still need to try Village Port.

Own Village Inn and Village Port expansions

RATING: 8
I didn't know much about Village until my girlfriend told me a story. She had it on her Amazon wishlist. One of her friends noticed this and told her "We have that at home, it never ever gets played - would you like it?". Resolutely refusing to take any money for it - we had a free game! I read the rules, watched a quick "how to play" and we were ready to go.

It didn't take long before both of us really got into this game! It's got so many mechanics we both love - being a well thought out worker placement game. It also has a mechanic that we hadn't seen before - your workers can die of old age! Wow, this is a great twist on the normal mechanisms of a worker placement. As well as dying, you also want them to die quicker than your opponent so they don't go into the unmarked graves! I love it when a game brings out a new mechanic I've never seen before.

Component wise, box is solid and all the pieces are quite functional. Sticking on the numbers to pieces is a little annoying but it's only a one-off, so something I can easily suffer through. What is great is that you get an entire extra set of stickers - just in case you manage to mess up with placing them. While I didn't need them, it was a great insurance to have! Artwork is very nice throughout the game.

All of the actions are well thought out and nothing seems particularly overpowered. The merchant maybe seems a bit underpowered/expensive to use, but this is a minor complaint. I wasn't overly happy with the travelling aspect of the game, but we've recently picked up the Port and Inn expansions. The Port expansion looks to really improve the travelling aspect of the game (I've heard it referred to as a "patch", not an expansion) so I'm looking forward to trying it out.

Overall, I think this is a superb Euro game and one that I'm exceptionally happy is now in my collection!

RATING: 6
25-07-15. Rated after 1 play. I am late to the village party, not being a euro gamer much.

In Village you take actions by grabbing cubes from places. These places give you actions, for which you can place a dude at the location.. signaling your 'glfamily' of meeples has a member (meeple) is doing that task (Traveling, beng clergy etc). Fun thing is that the game tries to simulate multiple generations and since time is a resource, your old meeples (lvl 1 meeples) die at some point and either lived rich and learning lIves and end up in a remembrance book.. or end up dead in an unmarked grave.  Funnily enough,  this is the endgame timer.

I did like my one play of it well enough, the mechanisms felt solid. However, I felt.the game was quite dry and methodical.For it's genre it's ok, but for myself I'd want something more.

RATING: 9
This game is unique and  really a fun game.You play a family that is  just living everyday life in the  villiage.  The object of the game is to become the most famous family in the village through your families generations. You can be famous for Exploring , entering government serving in the church etc. Your family members age and die off losing actions you can perform but can  get written into the history books for blazing a trail in their field scoring prestige points for your family.So a player needs to use their actions most productively while they are alive but still making sure your workers make an impression on the village when they pass away. I have not played anything like it.

Pros: Unique gameplay,  need to develped a balance approach, no harsh atttacking of other players in the game.Interesting theme and engaging game mechanisms.Beautiful artwork,Deep decisions making game  and strategy.

Cons: Gameplay may seem a little abstract

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Village may seem like standard worker placement fare, but it is much more than it appears at first glance. It contains one really novel idea: natural death of workers over time. This mechanic adds a great layer of strategy to the overall game, and it’s constantly a challenge to choose which workers will bite the dust first. Overall strategy seems fluid and adaptable: you don’t necessarily have to decide your main focus until round 2/3. This is a huge plus in my book, and it definitely avoided some frustration of having your strategy completely shut down through Caylus-esque blocking: someone can put his foot in your path, but not close the door. I also really like how the influence cubes are named and appropriately spent on activities that would need them, i.e. the brown faith cubes are used primarily at the church but also during travel.

The artwork in this game is absolutely phenomenal, and I really can’t say enough good things about it. In fact, it bumped my rating up by one full point. The style is subtle, practical and beautiful. Troyes was the last time I was impressed this much by the art. The bits are of high quality, and the rule book is excellent. Hopefully this game will get a wider distribution and earn the accolades it rightly deserves.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I absolutely love the concept of this. I'm more attached to the workers here than in any other game. Some have miserable and insignificant lives, others are triumphant point-scorers, but most will inevitably die to make way for the new generation. But it's not too morbid as the departed are usually honoured as pioneers (what a lovely message!). A changing pool of workers also makes your strategy seem more fluid -- your focal strategy naturally decays and you tend to move on to a new one. By design, all parts of the board will see action in every game, and the interconnectedness of each part rewards forward thinking and a well-rounded approach.

Yes the coloured cubes are silly and abstract but I really don't pay any attention to that. I do fault the game in that without an expansion it feels incomplete (and it is -- it was designed with Inn included before being surgically removed) and I would probably rate an 8. But if you can tap into the narrative theme of life and death you may just love Village as much as I do.

RATING: 7
Early impression: that the game is nice enough, but a little bit lacking in a certain spark, probably owing to the abundance of possible actions that have very close net end results. The test will come with replayability.

There's something fun about filling the grave slots (and interesting that that's just about the only blocking move available), and controlling when to kill the game is absolutely key.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Interesting how many people describe Village as "just another worker placement game", when in fact it is not your typical worker placement game at all. For starters, you are never required to place any workers to do your actions, and when the "workers" do get placed, it never blocks anyone else from taking the same action. It is better described as a resource gathering/action selection game, where the amount of resources out on the board constrain the number and types of actions players can take. 

Adding the Village Inn expansion for this game is definitely worth it and the replay value goes up significantly. The twists and turns of what cards become available in the inn force players to respond and adds a nice tactical element to the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Add Village Inn and you have a lot of options to score points.

RATING: 7.5
Interesting death mechanic where you score points for villagers that died based on that villager's profession. I do think replayability is somewhat limited which is why I did not give an 8+ rating, but I definitely enjoyed my first 3 plays of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Like the majority of current Euros, Village features a novel central mechanism tossed in together with a rehash of familiar side mechanisms. This isn't necessarily a bad thing, as the signature death mechanism is worth the price of entry alone. 

The death mechanism carries a few interesting implications. One, some careful timing is required to balance squeezing efficiency out of workers and earning a spot in the chronicle. Two, the game end is determined by the players, and a rash of deaths can see the game end faster than expected. Three, the impermanence of workers and competition to get a spot in the chronicle requires players to invest in different VP paths at different points of the game in order to do well. 

Unlike most middle-weight Euros, the pace of gameplay in Village is relaxed and resources are not as tight as one would imagine. After over 10 games, I've found that Village does not hold up well to repeat plays. There isn't enough variability in the set up or sufficient friction between players to ensure each game is a unique experience. 

So while Village is worth exploring for a game or two to experience the death mechanism, it doesn't have the staying power that keeps the game fresh after multiple plays.

Egyik olyan játék, amit látatlanban majdnem megvettem, így első játék után bánhatom, hogy nem tettem, mert jól ráéreztem, jó játék a Village. Tulajdonképpen két új dolgot lehet a játékban felfedezni. A legérdekesebb az akcióválasztó mechanizmus. A lehetséges akciókat ugyanis annyiszor lehet végrehajtani minden körben, ahány kocka rajtuk szerepel. Amikor valaki akciót akar végrehajtani egy adott helyen, onnan elvesz a még ott levő kockákból és utána hajthatja végre. A különböző színű kockák nem csak a számosságot mutatják, hanem különböző akciókhoz használhatók fel. A másik különlegesség a munkáslerakós stílust kicsit úgy dobták fel, hogy munkásaink idővel elhaláloznak és a temetőbe kerülnek. Adott számú sír betelte hozza el a játék végét is. Ez a két érdekesség, a gyönyörű kivitel és a jól kiegyensúlyozott akciók nagyon jó játékot adtak ki végül. Fanyalgásra csak a templomban levő szerencsés húzás adhat némi okot, de az pénzzel megoldható, illetve a játék végefeltételének nem könnyen kalkulálható volta. De ezek apróságok. Valószínűleg minden játékosszámmal jól működik a Village, bátran ajánlom a munkáslerakós stílus kedvelőinek.

RATING: 7
This game is a hoot. You could see it as a bland Euro, you're picking up cubes and then doing actions, sometimes paying cubes for them, sometimes paying other stuff, but the whole thing comes together into really innovative set of game systems, that makes the theme shine. The key mechanism is the aging/dying mechanism. Most stuff that you do ages you (it takes time!). Plague also ages you (duh!). Every certain number of aging steps a family member (worker) dies. Bummer, eh, but not totally. You're also trying to fit people into the Village chronicle, and dying at the right time in the right job can equal extra points.

I really like the various victory paths. I totally ignored travelling points and made most of my points trading, and won by 1 point! I haven't played enough to know if every path is viable but it feels like most should be. The game has some random elements but they are only moderate and shouldn't annoy any except the most anti luck people.

Now I need to try the expansions!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
1 play, 4er.

Wow, there is a lot going on in this game! I'm going to need to play this again, hopefully soon, to see if I can figure it out -- 'cause I sure didn't in this first game! Pretty enjoyable game, over all.

--- Summary ---
The board shows several locations where you can take actions such as, for example
- recruit an extra worker
- buy a plow, horse, ox, or wagon
- produce or sell grain
- place or advance a worker in the council (with better bonuses awarded as you advance further along the track; more VP at end of game, as well)
- place or advance a worker in the church (VP award at end of each turn and end of game)
- sell some combination of tokens for a VP chit (worth 3-6 VP and end of game)
- etc.
There are (at least!) 3 twists here:
1. At the start of each game turn, seed each location with a number of cubes (based on player count, and in a random assortment of colors drawn from a bag of cubes composed of a mix based on player count). To take an action, you have to take a cube in the appropriate location (if available). Many actions will require you to spend specific combinations of cubes.
2. Many actions also require some amount of time: advance your time marker on your personal track to show time passing. If you took a black cube in (1), you also advance 2 spaces on your time track.
3. Whenever your time marker makes a full trip around the track, you must kill off one of your workers from the earliest generation (start the game with gen-1 workers; as you recruit workers, you move through the generations) currently in play. The killed worker goes either to the Book of History (or whatever it's called), if there's still space for a worker of that type (specific number of spaces for church workers, councilmen, farmers, etc.); if not, go to the unmarked graves. The game ends when either the book or the graveyard is full. At the end of the game, you'll earn VP for having several workers in the Book.

When the game ends, you get additional VP for leftover money and cubes, as well as the VPs for being in the Book, and advancing in the Church and Council. Most VP wins.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
[b]Desire to play:[/b] high
[i](never/low/medium/high)[/i]

This is just a great Euro through-and-through. The game rules are easy to grasp, various strategies become apparent after a few rounds of play, the turns flow smoothly, and it plays in just the right amount of time, even at 5 players (i.e. it never feels like it overstays its welcome). 

As with most Euros, the player interaction isn't super high, the but competition for certain action spots can get pretty intense. It has a very similar feel to a worker placement game.

Sure, it's not super deep but it's just fun to play. The port expansion is almost essential in my opinion -- travel is too weak a strategy in the base game. The inn expansion adds more options, but isn't essential.

RATING: 8
2-4, 75m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A resource collection game with pseudo-worker placement elements.  In other words you could also title this game "Euro McEurogame".  I say "pseudo-" worker placement because at no time during the game are you ever required to place a worker on your turn and at no time does a worker being on a space block that space from being used by other players.

The main "gimmick" of this game is that time is another resource you must manage and your workers (family members) die off as time passes.  I put "gimmick" in quotes because while it was the main selling point of the game that set it apart from countless other Euros its more than just a gimmick.  It is a mechanic that actually works quite well.  At different points of the game you may be trying to delay or even accelerate the deaths of your family members since the deaths of these meeples also sets the tempo of the game.  The game ends when a certain number of meeples have died and you get points based on where and when they died.  At the same time you don't want the game to end before you've collected enough victory points other ways as well.

The action spaces seem well balanced and there's always that feature of action-taking Euros where you want to do 5 things in a round and only get to do 4.  This is a pretty good entry into a crowded game space that does a couple nifty new things.

RATING: 8
What a clever game!  Not quite a worker placement game, so much as an action selection game, each player is taking resource cubes off the board in order to place their workers and take action.  However, every action requires time, and as that time commodity runs out, the oldest generation of workers are taken from the board and either scored for points, or placed in a countdown timer to end the game.  There is a tension between wanting to keep your workers on the board to manufacture the resources you want, and wanting to place them in the scoring track.

RATING: 7
An excellent, smooth-playing, seemingly very well-balanced eurogame that epitomizes "multiple paths to victory". Unfortunately, like Hawaii, it's from the grab-bag school of game design: "elegant" is not the word. There are eight different things to do and none of them are quite alike; there are weird exceptions (you don't have to take the action with a cube, except for the market, where you do!). There is no theme.

Despite initially dismissing the game after reading the rules, I picked it up on the strength of the positive reviews. It really does play well, and I'd rate it higher if it had some exciting new mechanism at its heart (Macao's windrose, Trajan's mancala!).

Alas, while the dying meeples do add a little something, the time track is just another resource to calculate around -- it's less interesting than it sounds. The whole thing feels a bit soulless: solid but unexciting.

RATING: 8
Village is thematic for a Euro. About as thematic as Stone Age is. So, in other words, the things you do make sense as to why you do them. Setup is easy and it looks great.  It has lots of ways to make points. Yet, it has the agony of not enough actions to do everything you want. So you need to concentrate on a strategy at some point. It's forgiving but you need a little focus. That's why I like it so far. It has some exploration to do in the strategy. So far our games have us finding a different path to go on. It doesn't have a lot of soul but it's fun and looks good. Time will tell if it has legs.  

update : Each play makes me like it a little bit more.  The scoring in each game can be radically different depending on the paths players choose.  The ending is a little bit of a fizzle.  Count up points and see who did better.  There is not much of a story arc here.  Still, for a quick contest (between 2 anyways) it does a good job.  I like it with 2 quite a bit but 3 players feels more tense.

Also Expansion: Village Inn, Port

RATING: 8
Very interesting action selection mechanism! When you select an action to take, you take a cube from that place (if there are any left) and you do the action, but you also get the cube. Many times you need a cube of a certain color, but there is no guarantee you want to take an action where all those cubes are placed.

The aging aspect of the game also feels fresh and interesting. You actually want your meeples to die, so you can get points from them. Fresh ideas combined in a nice game that works. This is NOT a game where designers just put in as many mechanisms as they could to make it seem complex, this is a good one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Village is a pleasant, medium-weight worker-placement game. The villager death mechanic, which both requires and also encourages you to kill off some of your workers, is clever. It opens up a new path to victory points, and introduces a timing mechanic that is more thematic than any other worker placement game I've played (such as Tzolk'in). In the end, Village is still a resource collection and conversion game. The most efficient player will win. Player interaction comes in the form of denying other players actions, which is fine. Like most games of this type, however, I feel as though I will be heading down one of a few different paths each time I play with little room for variety. The different "stations" are more thematically integrated than in many other games I have played, and yet still feel disjointed (and even a bit fiddly). This is a function of the worker-placement genre more than anything else -- and so it is to be expected. Again, this is a clever game in the genre. It's simply not a genre I am overly enamored with.

RATING: 9
I'm enjoying this one in the early going. The only downside here is that if nobody decides to push the pace and spend time instead of resources, the four-player game can drag a bit. But with players who are familiar with the game, things shouldn't bog down, despite the many choices, as a turn simply consists of taking a cube and doing (or not doing) the associated action. There seems to be some depth here, and so far it looks like being a generalist - doing a little bit of everything - is just as effective as being a specialist - picking one method of scoring points and just pounding away at that area. I like that kind of game balance. Scores also seem to be competitive even when players use wildly divergent strategies. Some decisions are easy, but when the choice is between taking a useful resources from an area where you cannot perform the associated action or taking a bad black cube in an area where you can do the action, I'm still not sure which is better. I guess hard choices with no easy answers is part of why the game is interesting. I'm also happy to report that, while the game plays best with four, it works well as a two-player game, ensuring it will see some table time in-between meetings of my game group.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6

RATING: 8
Enjoyable Eurogame... Explaining it to new players is a bit of a cluster eff because the mechanics are so scattered, but the rules and mechanics are numerous more than complex... It all fits in eight the games core virtue, an impressively diverse scoring system that leaves you in the dark about who is the victor till everyone counts up points in the endgame. There are so many point sources and strategies to take, you can try for victory from almost any direction, the only obvious mistake is competing with another player too fiercely over one source of points compared to finding one neglected. The death and generations mechanic is particularly original and fun. A fine game, all in all.

RATING: 8.5
Resource management/Worker placement game with a nice and well integrated theme. and a twist: you can spend 'time' to make some actions but your workers will get older and eventually die!. I love how you can manage the 'time' to control the endgame. Easy to learn, low luck and deep gameplay. I love how you can specialize in some actions, which drive to several paths to win. Nice balance between strategic thinking (you can plan some turns ahead) and interactive/tactical play. Very good playtime for me (60-90min including setup and clean), well balanced, and the most important for us: It works great for 2 players. IMHO Village is one of the best midweight euros ever designed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.3
A 'reverse worker placement' game in which you take cubes to place workers, rather than placing workers to take cubes (as in Lords of Waterdeep). While the cubes are not as critical as they are in LoW - the actions are more important - the selection of available cubes can affect your choices. I love the aging mechanic, in which actions cost 'time' and eventually result in the death of your workers that have been in play the longest. The location of your meeples and the timing of their deaths can be very important. I very much prefer this to LoW.

RATING: 8
Life in the village is hard – but life here also allows the inhabitants to grow and prosper as they please. One villager might want to become a friar. Another might feel ambitious and strive for a career in public office. A third one might want to seek his luck in distant lands.

Each player will take the reins of a family and have them find fame and glory in many different ways. There is one thing you must not forget, however: Time will not stop for anyone and with time people will vanish. Those who will find themselves immortalized in the village chronicles will bring honor to their family and be one step closer to victory.

Village is a game full of tactical challenges. A smart and unique new action mechanism is responsible for keeping turns short and yet still tactically rich and full of difficult decisions. Also unique is the way this game deals with the delicate subject of death; as a natural and perpetual part of life in the village, thoughts of death will keep you focused on smart time-management.

RATING: 7
09/2012:7.5
This game is a worthy winner of Kennerspiel 2012. While in many ways, Village is a standard Euro-inspired game with various well-worn and timeless mechanics, the coupling of the time-track "death" mechanic with worker placement is relatively innovative. The board is crowded, but well-laid out and informative. The graphics and illustrations are also pleasing to the eye. 

Like many other complex Eurogames in this genre, players are presented with multiple paths to victory. Each path forces the player to expand limited resources to reach longer term, end-game goals (collecting prestige points in this instance). 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Nice elements in the game. I especially enjoyed the "take a cube, make an action mechanic". Also, theme was more stronger than in most modern games, which is a big plus. 

However, I would have liked the game to be a bit more compact. It is pretty long and there are many different aspects to the game - perhaps few too many for my personal euro-game resource management niche. I can see the first few games people are exploring the choices, but after that the game could start to feel samey. Personally i prefer games that push you to different directions and force you to explore new strategies - village does not do it too much. 

Oh yeah, and the seeding of the cubes and placing stickers really are quite fiddly and irritating things to do.

RATING: 5


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Provisional rating, one play.  Yet another twist on the tired worker-placement mechanism, and a good one.  The type and number of actions available in round are limited and common to all players. Though the benefit of most actions is dependent on an assigned worker, the action isn't exclusive i.e. multiple players can get the same benefit in a round (removing my major frustration with many wp games). Workers are typically assigned permanently to an action, though in many cases the player can alter the benefit received, though some "die" during the game, potentially being converted to victory points.  Action points are a currency in the game (actually several currencies, as it's paid in cubes of different colours), as is Time; Time is the game clock.  It's pretty, too.

RATING: 6
This one's another heavy Euro, and it doesn't even bother with any theme.  Where?  A village, of course!  When?  Medieval-y or sometime.  There's a little bit of Pillars of the Earth (the book, not the game) glorification of the common man, but mostly this is all about keeping the VP engine well-oiled.  In a genre that is getting increasingly crowded, both mechanically and thematically, there's not a lot about Village to make it stand out.  It's good, I suppose, but it's destined to be forgotten on the shelf in favor of the more interesting games out there.

RATING: 8

Workers age and die, which makes for difficult decisions on which actions to take, as it causes your older workers to drop off. Lots of decisions in a short time.

RATING: 6.5
UPDATE: Got boring after five consecutive plays. It's got a delightful theme, gorgeous graphic design, but it feels like pushing cubes around a spreadsheet. There's no grand narrative - you're not building a city or civilisation, improving your port, fighting a war or storytelling. You're filling a book with dead people while scoring victory points until an arbitrary end point. 

The Village Inn expansion adds new varied ways to fill a book with dead people until an arbitrary end point (you can have people die in the pub and bribe citizens with beer. Citizens are cards that give you points for stuff, or assorted actions).

In short, a mediocre euro with a cute theme.

*****

Based on one play, this is Trajan with a delightful family-generations theme and cube selection/time instead of a mancala. Since Trajan would benefit from more closely integrated mechanics, theme, and action selection other than the much-lauded-but-gets-old-quick mancala, it's a good game for non-grognards.

Needs more plays before I can tell if gets boring/broken. I understand why it won the Kennerspiel. The theme makes what would be a VP salad game a genuinely charming and accessible experience.

RATING: 6
This is a neat little worker placement game that doesn't play like a lot of the others that are already in the market.  I like the idea of there being no competition on the sites themselves, but instead with it coming from how many times a particular action can be taken.  Plus, the time element of your workers adds a measure of thinking to an already complex game, as you want to manage when you use a worker before it dies.  The usefulness of the different colors of cubes adds a layer of strategy to manage (though the publisher's choice of colors is colorblind-aggressive), and it all ties in neatly into a challenging set of rules.  The game feels like an abstract (that is, you'll probably feel like you're pushing cubes around a board than you will that you're living in a village), but it all works in a nice way.  There's a lot of decision-making and thinking to do throughout the game.

1 play: 4 players

RATING: 6
Village made a big splash when it was first released due to its unique "death" mechanism, a feature that limited the amount of actions your meeples could take before passing on and being removed from the game. That's certainly an interesting twist, but to be perfectly honest, it's about the only interesting thing going on in this game.

It's labeled as a worker placement game, but really, Village is more of a set collection game. Your workers are more of an expendable resource for generating goods than markers for blocking opponents, and often you will take actions at various spots on the board without having to place a worker at all. Many times you end up grabbing certain color combinations of cubes as your primary motivation, with their associated actions only being resolved as a weak afterthought.

The thematic integration with the game is poor as well. The idea of a village slowly growing through the hard work and dedication of its people across multiple generations simply doesn't shine through the game's fairly standard cube pushing. The market isn't very dynamic, the farms produce with little effort and traveling is accomplished simply by spending a random assortment of cubes, with little rhyme or reason for the color requirements. Also, the whole death gimmick is more of a timer for the game than a hurdle for the players. It's easily manipulated, and as the main selling point for the game, I found it to be quite a letdown.

I'm surprised Tom Vasel didn't set this on fire when he reviewed it.

Obviously I didn't love it, but I will play it again. Now that I have thrown away any expectations of an engrossing story arc, I'm pretty sure there is an interesting abstract here. Somewhere.

RATING: 8
[i]Rating with the Inn and Port expansions added to the base game (7/10 without the expansions)[/i].

A lovely medium-weight euro. Village has a very "organic" feel to it - actions taken by players tell a story, which is very unusual for a "cube pushing euro" like that.  The base game lacks a bit of variety, but it's all fixed by the expansions - Village gets much better with both Inn and Port added, they really elevate the whole game to another level. 

The only downside is that when players decide to "save time" at all costs the game may drag.

RATING: 6.8

Maybe one or two more games will raise the rating. So far, Village is a good but disappointing game

RATING: 7.5
Even though its classified as worker placement, the mechanics in this one are unlike anything else I've played. A lot of fun, once you get the rules down. Also nice that it works well with 2 players, without any extra 2-player rules like most games have. 
Village is a game full of tactical challenges. A smart and unique new action mechanism is responsible for keeping turns short and yet still tactically rich and full of difficult decisions. Also unique is the way this game deals with the delicate subject of death; as a natural and perpetual part of life in the village, thoughts of death will keep you focused on smart time-management.

RATING: 5
Based on one play:

The game has some nice innovative ideas, but overall I found it very overwhelming without offering much excitement. There was a lot to keep track of, especially the different point requirements. There are lots of different cubes and animals and items and resources and they're all used in specific ways all around the board. It's a slow, continuous struggle for points. But then again, it was my first game, so I had absolutely no idea what the best moves were. 

I ended up going for a combined market/church strategy, ignoring the harbor (we played with the harbor expansion) entirely. I was also a little annoyed at how difficult it was to get gold, which you would need for a captain to use the harbor in the first place, which is why I never went for that in the end.

Overall, I am not feeling it with this one here, as much as I love the mechanism of time as a resource and having your workers die.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.4
Village is a euro game that is full of theme.  For a worker placement game, that is an exciting aspect.  The fact that your workers can "die" and that in many cases, you want them to die leads to direct strategic and tactical choices.  I love the blend of strategy vs. tactics in this game.  Do you go to the market to ensure you buy what you want and to block people out of buying before they get the resources they need?  Or do you take that extra worker you need?  This does feel like a game that you need to specialize in something to maximize your score.  However, no one strategy is noticeably better than another.  If you enjoy worker placement games, this one feels unique and you should add it to your collection!

RATING: 9
Hard to really call this one a "worker placement" game... though I suppose the limited actions in the various spaces amount to about the same thing.  Certainly a very good resource conversion game with a unique time tracking and meeple dying (often for your benefit!) mechanic.  Despite the cube pushing feeling devoid of theme... the rest of the game just drips it so a net result of a very nice middleweight Euro that tells a decent story too. As I play the game more I'm finding the different paths to victory to be balanced and interesting to explore.

RATING: 8
Sort of a reverse worker-placement game (although there is a small amount of real worker-placement too).  Instead of placing a meeple on the action you want, you remove a resource cube from it; once all the cubes are gone, the round ends.

I was a little disappointed how minor the family death theme turned out to be; based on what I'd heard I thought it would be bigger.  Cooler.  Still a good game, it just didn't quite live up to my expectations.

RATING: 8
Your first move, you just have no idea where to start.  Thereafter, you have plenty of options, but you have collapsed the possibilities enough that the microturns of the game zip along.  Myriad choices, a singular feel, and control over how fast the game ends.  What's not to like?

RATING: 9
For someone who can be intimidated by a true-to-life, hours-long Eurogame, I find Village to be a refreshing take on the worker placement game. There's a LOT that you can make your workers do, but there's not enough to be overwhelming. I also think that the death mechanic for your workers makes things all the more refreshing - for instance, do you keep your worker outside of town to score more VPs, or do you let the worker die to help towards major end-of-game VPs in the history book? I like Village, and so should you.
